==PAGE:2==
Development of injection setup for experimental study of cavitation in customized
DI diesel nozzle tips while utilizing liquid gaseous fuels.
Author:
Jeppe Vilsbøll Larsen (student no. s180388)

Anders Ivarsson, Associated Professor, Mechanical Engineering, DTU

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This project studies a commercially available Common-rail injection system, focus-
ing on a solenoid injector’s functionality and mode of operation. With the intent of
switching out the fuel in such an injection system from Diesel fuel to liquid gaseous
fuels, such as liquid ammonia. As such, liquid gaseous fuels are studied in terms of
relevant physical properties and chemical compatibility between the fuels of interest
and some relevant materials. Based upon the study of the physical properties of liquid
gaseous fuels, an existing injection system is studied to solve some challenges faced
when switching from Diesel fuel to liquid gaseous fuels. All this was done to study the
cavitation of liquid gaseous fuels in customized design fuel nozzles. Therefore, a theory
behind the cavitation flow characteristics in nozzles was investigated to enable such a
study on cavitation in fuel nozzles. Furthermore, to study the effect internal geometric
parameters have on the internal flow of fuel nozzles, a method for measuring the internal
geometry of fuel nozzles was developed with reservations.
This project will conclude that many of the challenges faced when transitioning from
Diesel fuel to liquid gaseous fuels were addressed, and clear solutions were derived.
However, due to a lack of time, it was not possible to evaluate some of these solutions;
thus, future work exists to evaluate these solutions. Solutions that will enable a thorough
experimental study on cavitation in fuel nozzles.
ii

Preface
This Master thesis project was conducted by Jeppe Vilsbøll Larsen, (s180388), (email:
s180388@dtu.dk), in the period 1st of February 2024 to the 1st of July 2024. This
project was done in collaboration with the Institute of Civil and Mechanical Engineer-
ing at the Technical University of Denmark. This project was done with the intent
of completing a master of science in engineering degree in mechanical engineering.
This project has three supervisors: Anders Ivarson(aniv@dtu.dk), Mads Carsten Jes-
persen(macajo@dtu.dk), and Frederik Westlye(fredrik.westlye@man-es.com).
Looking back, I realize that this project would not have been possible without many
great people who helped me along the way. Knowing that I may not always be the easiest
person to work with, I would especially like to thank my supervisors, Anders and Mads,
who have been a great help to me and supported me all the way. I would also very much
like to thank some people who have endured me for the past half year. People such as
Thomas and Niels. Here, I would like to thank Thomas for lending me his office space
and Niels for letting me steal his workshop tools. I hope I may borrow them again in
the future. I promise I will try my best to put them back.
”Mann Tracht, Un Gott Lacht” - ”Man Plans and God Laughs”
- old Yiddish adage
Jeppe Vilsbøll Larsen
DTU Construct, 1st of July 2024
Technical University of Denmark
Department of Mechanical Engineering
Kongens Lyngby

==PAGE:16==
1
Introduction
For many years, globalization, and thus also global trade, has been a reality with
increasing demand. This has resulted in a significant amount of goods being transported
around the world. Commercial shipping by container ships accounts for between 80-
90% of this demand. In the last few years alone, around 11 billion tons of goods have
been shipped around the world by container ships. [59] Due to the large and further
increasing demand for the transport of goods via container ships, the size of the world’s
container ships has only gotten bigger. In the mid-1950s, a typical container ship could
carry about 1000 Twenty-foot Equivalent Units (TEUs). Today, this number has risen
all the way up to around 21.000 TEUs. [58].
Due to the size of container ships sailing the world today, there is a significant lim-
itation in the amount of propulsion technologies that can power these ships. Of these,
only two fully developed, operationally stable technologies exist that can provide the
amount of energy required to power an average container ship these days. These two
technologies are comparatively the internal combustion engine and nuclear energy ex-
traction. And since history has seen extensive nuclear disasters in the last 60 years, this
technology, as it is today, does not seem to gain momentum in today’s society. This
leaves internal combustion engine technology as the only drive to ensure energy tech-
nology for the propulsion of today’s container ships.
As such, container ships worldwide burn around 300 million tons of Heavy Fuel Oil
annually. This also corresponds to an energy consumption of 12.4EJ. [59] Together, the
combustion of this HFO accounts for up to three percent of the world’s greenhouse gas
emissions annually. With an increasing desire in society to reduce the climate footprint
society imprints today, there is a burning desire to make society ”climate-neutral” by
the end of the year 2050. This also includes a desire from the International Marine
Organization (IMO) to make an almost complete phase-out of emissions of Green House
Gases (GHG) in connection with commercial shipping of goods via container ships. [49]
This goal has led shipping companies to look for new fuel alternatives to HFO, with
the desire for conversion to a fuel that can potentially be produced via a climate-friendly
process and that can burn without emitting climate-harmful GHGs, such as CO, CO2,
NOx, SOx, soot particles, and other particulate matter.
To this end, there has been an increasing demand for alternative fuels such as methanol
and liquid gaseous fuels. Fuels such as liquid gaseous Dimethyl ether, liquid propane
gas, and especially liquid gaseous ammonia. Today’s exhaust gas treatment technolo-
gies, such as Exhaust Gas Recirculation (EGR) and Selective Catalyst Reduction (SCR),
give liquid gaseous ammonia a potential opportunity to burn clean and not emit any
harmful GHG.
However, such fuels present many challenges that will have to be addressed to even-
tually replace HFO with these liquid gaseous fuels.
1
==PAGE:17==
These challenges include how to deliver the fuel for an internal combustion engine.
Since liquid gaseous fuels have entirely different physical properties, see section 3, re-
placing HFO while using the same injection system for the injection of liquid gaseous
fuels will not be possible. In addition, and especially when using condensed ammonia
gas, there is also a perspective on the responsible handling of this fuel since this fuel is
toxic when in contact with large concentrations.
With the successful implementation of condensed gas fuels in an injection system,
there will also be a great demand for optimization of the injection to achieve the most
optimal combustion efficiency.
Thus, the purpose of this thesis is to investigate and potentially solve some of the
challenges that occur in a commercially available injection system when the fuel is
switched from commercial Diesel fuel, which is used in today’s Compression-Ignited
Internal Combustion engine, to liquid gaseous fuel, such as liquid gaseous ammonia.
2
==PAGE:18==
2
Commercial Common-Rail Injection System for Diesel
Fuel
To gain fundamental knowledge of current injection systems for the direct injec-
tion (DI) of fuel into a combustion chamber, a pivotal technology to study is a regular
common-rail (CR) injection system for the DI of Diesel into a compression-ignited (CI)
internal combustion engine (ICE). This technology is the most common fuel delivery
system in most Diesel cars today.
When running a CI engine, it is crucial to create the best fuel delivery system for the
engine that results in the best combustion efficiency under specific running conditions.
Developing good combustion efficiency means creating a smooth heat release rate for
the combusting fuel. As such, it is in the operator’s interest to create conditions in the
engine that minimize premixed combustion conditions and maximize diffusive flame/-
combustion conditions. Generating these conditions in a combustion chamber means
achieving good instant contact between fully evaporated fuel molecules and very hot
oxygen molecules. Hence, maximizing diffusive flame combustion conditions means
achieving good fuel evaporation in the combustion chamber and a good fuel-air mix-
ture. To achieve good and fast evaporation of the fuel delivered to the combustion
chamber means providing the fuel in as tiny droplets as possible, thus decreasing the
droplet evaporation time in the chamber. Countless studies have shown that increas-
ing the combustion chamber air density and the injection pressure at which the fuel is
delivered to the chamber will ultimately improve the atomization of the fuel spray in
the chamber. As such, the purpose of the DI fuel delivery system is to create, regulate,
and deliver high-pressure fuel to the combustion chambers. Since the power output
of a CI engine is controlled through the amount of fuel/chemical energy delivered to
the combustion chamber, and the fuel amounts delivered to the combustion chamber
are highly proportional to the injection pressure, having complete control over this pro-
cess is very important. This process has been studied heavily over many years and by
many institutions. One such institution is the ”Engine Combustion Network (ECN)”
(https://ecn.sandia.gov/), which will be discussed in more detail in section 2.6.
3
==PAGE:19==
2.1
Mode of operation for Diesel CR injection system
2.1
Mode of operation for Diesel CR injection system
Figure 2.1: Flow diagram of commercially avail-
able common-rail Diesel injection sys-
tem. [9]
When studying the commer-
cially used CR injection system
for Diesel in a typical four-stroke
CI engine, this system contains
the following main parts,
as
shown in Figure 2.1: (1) a fuel
tank, (2) a fuel filter, (3) a
high-pressure fuel pump, (4) a
Common-rail, with a proportion-
ally regulated check valve and
high-pressure sensor to measure
injection pressure, (5) fuel in-
jectors, being either piezoelectric
or solenoid operated, see section
2.2, and (6), an Engine Control
Unit(ECU). When in operation, the High-pressure pump, a 3-cylinder piston pump shar-
ing the same camshaft, will take in fuel from the fuel tank through the fuel filter, pres-
surize the fuel to injection pressure, and forward the high-pressure fuel to the common
rail. The fuel pump is required to deliver a constant high-pressure fuel flow to the rail,
ensuring that there is always a supply of fuel in the rail.
In the CR, high-pressure fuel will be collected. It is the functionality of the rail to
accumulate high-pressure fuel, such that when fuel flows to an injector, an insignificant
pressure drop in the rail is observed, thereby creating a continuous stable injection pres-
sure. This functionality is counterbalanced with its functionality to build up pressure
quickly when starting the engine. Thus, the desired specific volume of the rail is de-
termined from these two functionalities. Using an electrically controlled proportional
check valve with a pressure sensor will create, together with the ECU, a closed regulat-
ing loop to control the fuel injection pressure in the CR. This ensures that less precise
control of the high-pressure pump’s flow outlet is necessary.
With a continuous stable high-pressure fuel supply accumulated in the common-rail,
fuel injectors are coupled to the CR and placed in the cylinder head of the engine, with
nozzle tips exposed to the cylinder [24].
The overall functionality of the injectors is to deliver very fine atomized, high-
pressure fuel droplets to the combustion chamber in precisely timed injection durations,
thus creating conditions for high combustion efficiency and low emissions in the ex-
haust. The injectors in a CR injection system will be described in more detail in section
2.2 [24].
4
==PAGE:20==
2.2
Bosch solenoid injector
2.2
Bosch solenoid injector
Figure 2.2: Cross-sectional view of Bosch solenoid
injector. For a more detailed view of the
original injector and all the components
in it, see appendix B.
Having briefly described the
functionality of a commercial
common-rail system and its mode
of operation, a literature study
of the functionality and mode
of operation of a commercially
used Bosch Diesel injector is pre-
sented [24]. Many injector de-
signs exist for directly inject-
ing Diesel fuel into a cylinder
chamber. The most common are
the piezoelectric and solenoid-
driven injector designs, with the
solenoid injector design being
the oldest and used by the ECN
group (see section 2.6).
Thus,
for this paper, the focus is on the
Bosch solenoid injector, specif-
ically the Bosch solenoid injec-
tor with part number ”0 986 435
091” [4].
Based on the functionality of
the injector described in the pre-
vious section, the design of the
solenoid injector can be subdi-
vided into three main subassem-
blies, each contributing to the
overall functionality of the injec-
tor. These subassemblies are the
servo valve subassembly on top
of the main injector body (see
Figure 3(a)), the main injector
body (see Figure 2.2), and the in-
jector nozzle subassembly below
the main injector body (see Fig-
ure 3(b)).
When observing the injec-
tor’s mode of operation from the
perspective of the high-pressure
5
==PAGE:21==
2.2
Bosch solenoid injector
(a) Servo valve and solenoid head subassembly
on top of the main injector body.
(b) Nozzle subassembly mounted under the main
injector body.
Figure 2.3: Cross-sectional views of sub-assemblies on the Bosch solenoid driven in-
jector. Here, the red color represents the surface that is exposed to high-
pressure fuel, the green-colored surface represents the surface that is ex-
posed to low-pressure fuel going to the return line, and the cyan color
represents the components making the solenoid armature/anchor assembly.
These components are also the components that travel when the injector is
activated.
.
fuel, the fuel to the injector is
supplied from the high-pressure
fuel inlet fitting. Here, the high-
pressure fuel is split into two lines: one operates the servo valve assembly, and the other
is the main fuel supply line to the nozzle.
In the main fuel supply line, the fuel travels down the tube in the main injector body
and arrives at the nozzle body. Here, the fuel is sent to the manifold around the nozzle
needle and distributed evenly before arriving at the nozzle needle seat. This prevents
the fuel from entering the nozzle body sac volume when the injector is not activated.
In the line to the servo valve assembly, the fuel arrives at the high-pressure fuel
manifold around the part making up the valve control chamber. From the manifold, the
fuel passes through a small hole into the valve control chamber and up a small hole in the
top of the valve control chamber, where a servo valve ball seals/closes the valve control
chamber when the injector is not activated. Due to very fine mechanical tolerances, the
component that makes up the valve control chamber and the injector main body plunger
creates a hydraulic seal that ensures high-pressure fuel does not flow to the low-pressure
6
==PAGE:22==
2.2
Bosch solenoid injector
fuel side in the return of the injector main body.
When high-pressure fuel meets the servo valve ball in the valve seat, a hydraulic
force acts on it. Thus, when the injector is not activated, a counterforce has to act on the
ball to seal the valve control chamber. This counterforce comes from the solenoid spring
valve that acts on the solenoid armature/servo valve anchor axis and transmits the force
to the servo valve ball, see parts colored cyan in Figure 3(a). Knowing the hydraulic
injector pressure (approximately 2000 bar), the solenoid valve spring is typically pre-
loaded with a force 15 percent greater than the hydraulic force acting on the servo valve
ball [24].
When the injector is not activated, a hydraulic force balance over the plunger is
located in the main injector body. See Figure 2.2. Here, a hydraulic force from the high-
pressure fuel in the valve control chamber counteracts the hydraulic force acting on the
pressure shoulder on the nozzle needle. See Figure 3(a). Thus, when not activated, the
pre-loading force from the nozzle needle spring helps seal the fuel from entering the sac
volume of the nozzle body under the nozzle needle tip.
In summary, when the injector is not activated, a set of force balances over the
solenoid armature/servo anchor axis, injector plunger, and nozzle needle, together with
preloaded springs, helps keep the injector closed.
When activating the injector for an injection event, high current and voltage are sup-
plied to the solenoid coil through the plastic cover on top of the injector. The solenoid
coil then creates a magnetic field force that acts on the anchor plate, which is positioned
with a very small air gap between the anchor plate and the solenoid coil body (approxi-
mately 0.05 mm when the injector is closed and 0.01 mm when the injector is activated).
A typical two-phase current profile is used for normal injection operation. In the first
phase, a pulse current is supplied to the solenoid coil to facilitate a fast reaction of the
anchor axis. This initial current is typically in the range of 15-20A at 40V for 0.3ms.
Subsequently, in the second phase of the current profile, the solenoid coil is given a
lower current in the range of 10-15A at 40V for the remaining duration of the injection
event. The time average load of the solenoid coil is typically around 75W [21].
To ensure the correct distance is maintained between the solenoid coil body and the
anchor plate, a less powerful spring, the overstroke spring, is mounted under the anchor
plate [3]. Based on this magnetic force, the solenoid spring force is overcome, causing
an imbalance in the force balance over the servo valve anchor axis, allowing the anchor
axis to move freely.
As the anchor axis travels upward, the servo valve ball is lifted from its seat, creating
a fuel flow passage between the valve control chamber and the valve seat. This allows
high-pressure fuel to flow through the valve.
The flow passage between the high-pressure manifold around the valve control cham-
ber and the valve control chamber itself is smaller than the passage between the valve
control chamber and the valve seat, causing a decrease in hydraulic pressure in the valve
7
==PAGE:23==
2.3
Force balances in solenoid injector
control chamber. This disrupts the force balance over the injector plunger since the
counterforce from the valve control chamber becomes less than the hydraulic force act-
ing on the pressure shoulder of the nozzle needle. Consequently, the hydraulic force on
the pressure shoulder of the nozzle needle can overcome the preloaded force from the
nozzle spring. This allows the nozzle needle to travel, creating a flow passage from the
evenly distributed fuel around the nozzle needle down to the nozzle body sac volume
under the nozzle needle tip.
In summary, activating the solenoid creates a disturbance in the force balance over
the anchor axis, main injector body plunger, and nozzle needle, allowing the nozzle
needle to travel. Thus, fuel injection into the combustion chamber becomes possible.
2.3
Force balances in solenoid injector
Figure 2.4: The forces acting on the anchor assembly colored in cyan. The black arrows
describe the positive directions.
After having studied the mode of operation of a Bosch solenoid injector, it was found
relevant to study the forces acting on the servo value anchor axis. As such, a kinematic
force balance was derived, as shown in equations 2.1.
m˙v = Fhyd,ball + Fos + Fmagnet + Fnormal −Fsol,spring −Fg
(2.1)
Where m is the total mass of all the component that travels with the anchor axis, Fhyd,ball
is the hydraulic force acting on the servo valve ball, Fmagnet, is the magnetic force from
the solenoid coil, Fsol, is the force from the solenoid spring acting on the anchor axis,
Fos is the force of the overstroke spring acting on the anchor plate, Fnormal is the normal
8
==PAGE:24==
2.3
Force balances in solenoid injector
surface force on the servo valve ball when the injector is not activated, and Fg is the
total gravitational force acting on the armature/anchor subassembly.
Knowing that there is preloaded tension in the spring when the injector is not acti-
vated, equation 2.1 can be rewritten to split this action up:
m˙v(t) = Fhyd,ball + Fos,preload + Fos(s(t))
+ Fmagnet + Fnormal −Fsol,preload −Fsol(s(t)) −Fg
(2.2)
Where Fos,preload is the preloading force in the overstroke spring, Fos,spring(s(t)) is the
excess force in the overstroke spring when the injector is activated, Fsol,preload is the
preloaded force in the solenoid spring, and Fsol,spring(s(t)) is the excess force in the
solenoid spring, when the injector is activated.
Thus, when analyzing the forces in the injector, three states of equation 2.1 exist. The
first state where the injector is not activated. In this senario: Fmagnet = 0, Fos(s(t)) = 0
and Fsol(s(t)) = 0, Fnormal takes the value as shown in the following equation:
Fnormal = Fhyd,ball + Fos,preload −Fsol,preload −Fg
(2.3)
As such, equation 2.3 also describes the net magnetic force needed to overcome the
preloaded tension of the springs for the servo valve to open.
Using the general equations for the force in a spring Fs = ks, where k is a spring
constant, and s is a distance, As well as, the definition of a hydraulic force being:
Fhyd,ball = pinj

4 , a differential equation can be derived to describe the travel of the
anchor axis when in motion.

Where pinj is the injection pressure in the valve control chamber, dball is the diameter of
the servo valve ball, k is a spring constant, sp, is the preload compression distance for
a spring, s(t) is the distance the anchor axis has traveled as a function of time. [24]
Knowing differential equations to describe the motion of the anchor axis, a set of
boundaries/restrictions for the movement of the anchor axis can be defined. Assuming
that the anchor axis has the position of s(t) = 0, when the servo valve ball is sitting in
its seat, a clear restriction of the anchor axis is that unless the magnetic force is greater
than or equal to the normal force or static force of the system, Fmagnet ≥Fnormal, the
resulting acceleration of the anchor axis is zero. ˙v = 0
Furthermore, the same type of restriction can be set when the anchor axis has traveled
the maximum height(smax). Thus, when the magnetic force is larger than the normal
force, and the travel distance is equal to smax, the resulting acceleration of the anchor
axis should also be zero. Otherwise, when the anchor axis position is in between zero
and smax, the motion of the anchor can be described using equation 2.4.
Using these boundaries/restrictions, the motion of the anchor axis can be described
using differential equation 2.5

better understanding for the magnetic force in the servo valve, the reader is refered to
section 11.

==PAGE:31==
2.6
Engine Combustion Network
2.6
Engine Combustion Network
To better understand the commercially used fuel injection system and what parame-
ters are used to create better combustion efficiency in engine spray combustion, exten-
sive research has been performed over the years to increase knowledge of the combus-
tion process and better regulate it for a desired design need. To this end, the website
Engine Combustion Network was created by Sandia National Laboratories in 2011 and
has over 53 active research institute participants. Using this domain, researchers from
different research institutes worldwide can share their experimental research data to bet-
ter create valid, reproducible, and cost-efficient simulations of the combustion process
in any design situation. Hence, the combustion process in an engine can be improved
to achieve better energy efficiency and lower concentrations of unwanted emissions.
To facilitate better collaboration between the different research institutional partici-
pants, the ECN community has streamlined the experimental research conditions setup
and facilities to get valid and reproducible results. To this end, standards regarding the
experimental setup and procedures have been developed. These standards relate, among
other things, to the experimental equipment and conditions under which Diesel spray ex-
periments must be carried out. To understand these conditions, the conditions made by
Sandia National Laboratories have been studied. See section 2.7.
2.7
Sandia experimental setup and spray conditions
To study the first edition of the experimental setup created to investigate Diesel spray
combustion, an article from [61] was investigated. Using this article, it was found that a
cylindrical formed constant volume combustion chamber(CVCC) was utilized to study
Diesel spray combustion. see Figure 2.11. This chamber has several important features,
(a) Schematic of CVCC
(b) Experimental test stand
Figure 2.11: Experimental setup of Constant Volume Combustion Chamber at Sandia
National Laboratories in 1985. [61], [29]
such as inlet plugs and an optical glass view to inspect the Diesel spray formation and
16
==PAGE:32==
2.7
Sandia experimental setup and spray conditions
combustion. Based on [61], the plugs are used to install the following: (1) fuel injec-
tor, (2) inlet valve, (3) exhaust valve, (4) Mixing fan, (5) spark plug, and (6) Pressure
transducer. Utilizing a CVCC, it is possible to regenerate the temperature, pressure,
and chamber chemical composition conditions that are typically seen at the top dead
center(TDC) condition in an operational CI engine.
Before injecting the experimental fuel spray, a premixed combustion event was cre-
ated to generate the temperature, pressure, and chamber chemical composition con-
ditions seen at TDC conditions in an engine. To make the TDC conditions a well-
controlled composition mixture of Acetylene, Hydrogen, Oxygen, and Nitrogen gas is
injected through the inlet valve into the CVCC. Using the mixing fan in the chamber,
this mixture is fully mixed before igniting it by the spark plug in the chamber, thereby
creating a well-controlled premixed combustion event. Well-controlled in the sense that
by determining the target exhaust gas composition of the premixed combustion process,
the composition of the reactant gas mixture to make this premixed combustion process
can be calculated using the target exhaust gas composition. This calculation is done un-
der the assumption of no condensation of water vapor in the exhaust gas. The chamber
was preheated to 432 K to ensure this assumption.
After a certain well-controlled cool-down period of the premixed combustion ex-
haust gasses, conditions are reached mimicking the temperature and pressure conditions
equivalent to TDC conditions. Thus, experimental fuel spray is ready to be created by
injection of fuel into the CVCC. See Figure 2.12. A pressure transducer was installed
Figure 2.12: A typical pressure history in a CVCC for the experimental investigation
of a Diesel spray, at Sandia 1985 [61].
in the CVCC to study the Diesel spray event. Furthermore, sapphire glass windows
in both flat faces of the cylindrical chamber allowed the installation of optical instru-
ments, including light sources and cameras, thus obtaining optical data of the Diesel
spray event.
17
==PAGE:33==
2.8
Diesel spray operation conditions
Based on this experimental setup equipment, a standardized way of investigating
Diesel spray injection events was generated, which several institutional participants
have adopted since then.
2.8
Diesel spray operation conditions
To easily compare experimental results from different institutional participants and
thereby get an idea of the validity and reproducibility of experimental results between
participants, ECN has identified/made standards for the operating conditions for inves-
tigating Diesel spray events for all institutional participants who want to participate in
ECN. [30] Here, ECN has identified four different Diesel spray operation conditions,
spray A through D. These conditions will be explained in more detail in the following
sections.
2.8.1
Spray A & B
The first CVCC operation conditions created by the ECN groups are the so-called
”spray A conditions.” These conditions should be the equivalent of a low-temperature
combustion event that would be typical for engines running with a mild Exhaust Gas
Recirculation(EGR) system. Hence, they have a very low, if any, concentration of NOx.
[55]. These operating conditions specify conditions for the nozzle geometry and ambient
gas conditions wherein the Diesel spray event should occur. To see the ambient gas
conditions, see table 2.4. To test these conditions, Bosch GmbH initially donated 10

Spray C & D
To study the effect cavitation in the nozzle has on the Diesel spray event in the

3
Liquid Gaseous Fuels
3.1
Physical properties
After having studied the commercially available CR injection system using Diesel,
see section 2.1, an investigation into the properties of liquid gaseous fuel should be
made to gain better knowledge on the influence alternative fuels, such as liquid gaseous
fuels, will have on the commercial available CR injection system. This investigation
was made by extraction of physical properties from the Software Engineering Equation
Solver(EES). [27]
Based on the mode of operation of the hydraulically operated Bosch solenoid injec-
tor, where the injection pressure acts as a hydraulic force on the pressure shoulder of the
nozzle needle, and considering that the nozzle needle and the injector main body plunger
have very fine mechanical tolerances to minimize fuel leakage to the low-pressure side
of the injector, it is important to understand the influence of alternative fuels on these
functionalities. Three physical properties of alternative fuels have been identified as key
indicators of their influence on the solenoid injectors in this regard. These properties
are: {(1)} the saturation pressure of the liquid or gaseous fuels, {(2)} the viscosity of
the fuels, and {(3)} the isothermal compressibility of the fuel.
3.1.1
Saturation pressure
0

Figure 3.1: The saturation pressure for different alternative fuels, as a function of tem-
perature. N-dodecane is used in this regard as a representative fluid for
Diesel, and n-octane is used as a representative fluid for gasoline.
21
==PAGE:37==
3.1
Physical properties
To operate any high-pressure pump and achieve the same injection pressures as with
Diesel, it is necessary to ensure that the high-pressure pump has an unrestricted supply
of liquified fuel. Due to the increased saturation pressure of the liquid, gaseous fuels,
see Figure 3.1; it is necessary to improve the low-pressure side of the injection system
to fulfill This requirement. Should these requirements not be fulfilled, in many cases
where the high-pressure pump in the injection system is a piston pump design, the pump
will experience a vapor lock. This is a state wherein the pump only takes in fuel in a
gaseous state instead of a liquid state. In this state, the pump stroke will only be used to
compress the fuel in a gaseous state and not create high-pressure liquid fuel for delivery
to the high-pressure side of the injection system.
Furthermore, because the low-pressure side of any injection system has to be oper-
ated at higher pressures, this action can increase the potential for the fuel to cavitate in
the injection system. According to Bernoulli’s principle, [6] this increased potential for
cavitation will be present wherever there is an acceleration of the fuel in the injection
system, resulting in local pressure drops. Hence, should the local pressure of the fuel fall
below the saturation pressure, cavitation will occur. Having studied the commercially
available CR injection system and the Bosch solenoid injector, it can be foreseen that
such increased potential will be present in the check valves of the high-pressure pump,
see section 7.2 in the proportional regulating valve in the CR, as well as in the solenoid
injector, especially in the nozzle hole of the nozzle body. This will be further studied in
section 4.
3.1.2
Viscosity
Based on general fluid mechanics, [6], it is known that there is a general connection
between the viscous properties of a fluid and the potential pressure drop in passages.
This connection can be shown using the system of equations 3.1-3.3.

very fine mechanical tolerances to minimize leakage will greatly be influenced by a fluid
with a lower viscosity. Therefore, it was deemed necessary to investigate the difference
in viscosity between Diesel fuel and liquified gaseous fuels. This Investigation can be
shown in Figure 3.2. Based on Figure 3.2, it can clearly be derived that using an alter-
native liquid gaseous fuel, such as ammonia, Dimethyl ether(DME), or liquid propane
gas(LPG), will potentially increase the amount of leakage present from the valve control
chamber and into the low-pressure side in the injector main body around the plunger.
Furthermore, increased leakage may also be seen over the nozzle needle and into the
injector main body.
Knowing there to be an increase in the amount of leakage of high-pressure fuel in
the Bosch solenoid injector, no guarantee was present on whether the pumping capacity
of the high-pressure pump in an injection system is enough. The reader is referred to
section 9 for further study on this topic.

3.1.3
Isothermal compressibility
Due to Diesel fuel’s low compressibility, a high-pressure piston pump is well suited
for creating high-pressure fuel in an injection system. Because of this compatibility, it
is possible to quickly build up pressure in the pump cylinder, as very little displacement
volume is needed to achieve the desired pressure. As a result, most of the stroke volume
is used to pump high-pressure fuel and not just to compress it.
Utilizing a fuel with higher compressibility, more displacement volume in the pump
has to be used to overcome the desired pressure, and less displacement volume will be
used to pump fuel. In summary, the pumping capacity will be lowered. It was, therefore,
necessary to investigate the compressibility of liquid gaseous fuels. The result of this
investigation can be seen in Figure 3.3. Here, it is clearly seen that changing the fuel
to a liquid gaseous fuel will most likely lower the pumping capacity of a pump in an
injection system, should the pump be of a piston pump design. When factoring in the
increased leakage due to a lower viscosity, it is seen as even more important to optimize
the amount of the original pump capacity available.
24
==PAGE:40==

Having investigated the chemical compatibility between the fuels of interest and

==PAGE:41==
3.2
Chemical compatibility of liquid gaseous fuels
When analyzing table 3.1, it can also be shown that Teflon(PTFE) and Kalrez@(FFKM)
is an excellent sealing gasket material. Through [40], it can clearly be shown that these
materials are also the most expensive. Therefore, when implementing sealing gaskets
into the injection system, it may be beneficial to provide different sealing gasket mate-
rials for the same seal, and switch the sealing gasket depending on the type of fuel that
should be used in the system.
In summary, when considering the Diesel fuel as a reference fuel, it can clearly be
stated that liquid gaseous fuels have significantly different physical properties. Proper-
ties that are foreseen to have an extensive effect on the functionality of the commercially
available CR injection system.

==PAGE:42==
4
Cavitation in Nozzle Holes
After studying the changes in the physical properties of liquid gaseous fuels and
the fuel’s chemical compatibility, the focus will now be shifted toward their effects on
the nozzle hole orifice and, thereby, the flow characteristics of the fuels in the nozzle.
To this end, some theories will be presented in the following section. This theory will
lay the foundation for the theory that can be used to design nozzles and experimentally
hydraulically characterize existing fuel nozzles.
4.1
Reynolds Transport Theorem
In general, when analyzing the fluid flow of a simple system, the Reynolds transport
theorem is a good place to start. Using this theorem, it is possible to link a control volume
with a system of a fluid. See Figure 4.1.

it has been shown that at small distances from the nozzle hole orifice, entrainment of
ambient air into the fuel spray helps with conserving the momentum flux of the spray,
since kinetic energy is transferred between the liquid droplets and the ambient entrained
gas in the spray.

4.4
Discharge coefficient as a function of the cavitation number
Having defined the discharge coefficient of the flow through the nozzle hole, it is
desirable to link the discharge coefficient to a so-called cavitation number. [48]
The fuel flow in the nozzle hole is analyzed to perform this linkage. See Figure
4.2. When analyzing a non-cavitation flow in the nozzle hole, due to high pressure, the
fluid will separate from the nozzle hole wall at the beginning of the hole due to the large
shift in flow direction, creating core flow conditions. See Figure 4.2. After the core, the
flow will reattach to the nozzle hole walls until the opening to the nozzle hole outlet is
reached.
Assuming that the flow is ideal and losses only occur in the section between the
core and the outlet, Bernoulli’s principle can be used to describe the transition of the
inlet conditions to the core conditions:
p1 = pcore + 1

(4.25)
p1 is the injection pressure at the inlet to the nozzle hole, and vcore is the ideal velocity
at the nozzle hole core.

Nurick et al. showed that radius ratios over 0.14 do not affect the contraction ratio and,

In summary, a relationship between the discharge coefficient and the cavitation num-
ber K = p1−pvap
p1−pB was derived. Here, it is clearly shown that the vapor pressure of the
fuel can influence the amount of cavitation in the nozzle. Thus, when introducing a
liquid gaseous fuel, due to the increase in vapor/saturation pressure, there is a real risk
of increasing the amount of cavitation occurring in the nozzle hole, potentially lowering
the discharge coefficient of the nozzle.
Furthermore, having a clear expression that can potentially estimate the experimen-
tal mass flow through the nozzle based on easy measurable parameters is a strong tool.
To this end, it will be recommended for future work to further develop this model to ac-
count for the fluids viscous properties, by potentially making the contraction coefficient
a function of either the viscosity or the Reynolds number.

5
Design and Selection of Fuel Nozzles
Having described the dependency between some of the nozzle geometric parameters
and the hydraulic operation of a fuel nozzle, where the nozzle orifice is of great interest,
it became necessary to choose which Diesel nozzles should be used to study the effect
of changing the fuel type from Diesel fuel to an alternative liquid gaseous fuel. See the
section 3.1 and 4 to understand the potential difference. To this end, it was chosen to
follow two paths. One path where we utilize existing nozzles in-house, and another path
where we potentially design customized nozzles and get them manufactured by Robert
Bosch GmBH.
5.1
Existing nozzles
Based on the present in-house nozzles that were available from previous studies us-
ing Diesel, also made by the author, [33], [34]. Two single-hole nozzles from Robert
Bosch GmBH were used. Those having the part number: ”B433206397”, and ”B433206394”.
See Figure 5.1. Based on the present in-house nozzles that were available from previ-
Figure 5.1: Orifice comparison in a microscope, nozzle ”B433206394” on the right,
and ”B433206397” and the left. [33]
ous studies using Diesel, also made by the author, [33], [34]. Two single-hole nozzles
from Robert Bosch GmBH were used. Those having the part number: ”B433206397”,
and ”B433206394”. See Figure 5.1. It was assumed that the two nozzles have the
same geometry, though it is clearly shown in Figure 5.1, that nozzle ”B433206394”
is much more worn than nozzle ”B433206397”. As such, it was chosen to use nozzle
”B433206397” and install this on the selected injector in the experimental setup. A
more detailed description of this nozzle was made in the previous works done by the
author [33], where a description of how the nozzles are cleaned and installed on the
injector was described. Assuming that the two nozzles have the same geometry, efforts
were made to characterize the internal geometry of nozzle ”B433206394”. The reader
is referred to section 6 for a complete description of how this was performed.

5.2

5.2
Design of customized nozzles
5.2.1
Collaboration with Bosch GmBH
After many years of contact with several partners within the Robert Bosch GmBH(Bosch)
concern, it became possible to establish a collaboration with Bosch to create our own
customized single-hole nozzles. Based on the offer presented by Bosch, it was chosen
to design three customized nozzle designs and buy 100 of each design.
To this end, the three nozzle designs were chosen to resemble those of spray A, C,
and D in the ECN group. This choice will make it possible to share experimental results
with the ECN group. As shown in section 2.6, each of the three nozzle designs has
specific requirements for the internal geometry of the nozzle regarding the nozzle hole
diameter, nozzle hole k-factor, nozzle shaping, and many other geometric requirements.
Here, the most challenging parameter to specify was the nozzle shaping. When
analyzing table 2.5, and table 2.7, it is seen that for the nozzle shaping of spray A and
D, a discharge coefficient was specified for the rounding of the inlet to the nozzle hole.
In contrast, the spray C nozzle’s five percent hydro-erosion process was defined.
Due to the authors’ limited knowledge of the hydro-erosion process, it was chosen to
make a literature review on this topic. [26], [23], [25]. Based on this literature review, it
was found that hydro-erosion of the inlet of the nozzle hole is a grinding process where
an abrasive fluid is injected through the nozzle to grind the entrance to the nozzle hole,
thus rounding the inlet. It was derived here that the amount of hydro-erosion/hydro-
grinding is measured based on the relative change in the mass flow of the nozzle at the
same injection conditions. See equation 5.1. [26]
DegreeHG[%] = ˙mafter −˙mbefore
˙mbefore
· 100
(5.1)
A mixture of oil and very fine aluminum oxide grains is injected through the nozzle
at approximately 10 MPa to hydro grind the inlet to the nozzle hole. Here, the amount
of mixture injected through the nozzle is measured to derive the change in the mass
flow rate through the nozzle. An oil with many of the same physical properties, such
as viscosity and density, is chosen to ensure good representation between the abrasive
grinding fluid and Diesel fuel. This good representation makes it possible to predict a
discharge coefficient of the nozzle using the abrasive grinding fluid.
Based on the theory described in section 4, and equation 5.1, a code script in the
software Engineering Equation Solver(EES) was generated to calculate the degree of
percent Hydro-erosion necessary to achieve a discharge coefficient as specified for spray
A and D conditions. Using the physical properties of n-Dodecane at T = 298 K, the
values for the presumed discharge coefficient and degree of hydro-erosion between the
three nozzles can be seen in table 5.1.

6
Characteristics of Internal Nozzle Geometry
After determining which nozzles should be used in the study being ”B433206394”
for measurements, efforts must be directed towards analyzing and characterizing the
actual internal flow geometry, as described in Section 4. Different internal nozzle flow
geometries can drastically affect flow formation regarding discharge coefficient, area
contraction coefficient, etc. To separate the effect internal geometric parameters have on
the flow characteristics and the fuel spray formation, knowing the internal geometry of
the nozzles is paramount. In the initial process of characterizing the nozzles, a literature
review of previous studies on measuring the internal geometry of DI Diesel nozzles was
conducted. See Section 6.1.
6.1
Previous studies on the methodology of measuring the internal
geometry of direct injection Diesel nozzles
When exploring previous efforts to measure the internal geometry of direct injection
Diesel nozzles, very few methods exist. Here, the methodologies are divided into two
categories: destructive and non-destructive.
Studying the work previously done to measure the internal geometry utilizing de-
structive methods, this is mainly done by splitting a nozzle into two halves and using
one half in a microscope to create a symmetric profile of the internal geometry of a noz-
zle. Such a method has its advantages since the certainty of the validity of the measured
results is present. Furthermore, having the internal nozzle surface in hand would make a
higher resolution in the microscopical scan possible. Parameters such as surface rough-
ness can be determined. The disadvantage of such a measuring method is that they are
destructive, rendering the nozzle useless after measuring the internal geometry.
Several methods exist when studying non-destructive methodologies. Here, two of
the most popular methods are optical methodologies, such as X-ray computed tomogra-
phy(CT) [25], [32] and the creation of silicone mold replicates of the internal geometry
of the nozzles, and then make microscopical scans of the silicone mold replica. [38].
Both methods have advantages and disadvantages.
While X-ray CT has its advantages in that measurements are again made directly on
the nozzle, the certainty of validity and reliability of the measurements is present. The
disadvantage of this method is the very high cost. Due to the cost of renting regular X-ray
machinery. Here, further complications exist due to the metal thickness of the nozzles.
Due to the metal thickness present in regular Diesel nozzles, high-energy X-ray CT is
not strictly required but is very much recommended to get an acceptable contrast and
resolution between the metal in the nozzle and the air in the hole in the nozzle. The
author also has previous experience utilizing X-ray CT. [32]. The author could measure
the hole’s internal diameter using the X-ray CT methodology. Still, due to the limitations
42
==PAGE:58==
6.2
Internal geometric parameters of interest
in the amount of energy generated in the scans, limitations on the contrast and resolution
were present, rendering some questions about the reliability of the measurements. Also,
due to the relatively low energy levels of the X-ray CT, it was only possible to measure
the internal hole diameter and none of the other internal geometric parameters of interest.
See section 6.2 for a description of all the internal geometric parameters of interest.
When looking at the advantages and disadvantages of creating silicone mold repli-
cates of the internal geometry of the nozzles, some of the most obvious benefits are the
low cost and easy execution. Utilizing a customized tube to be inserted into the inter-
nal geometry of the nozzle provides direct accessibility to the sac volume and holes in
the nozzle. One of the more severe disadvantages of this methodology is the validity
of the silicone mold replicate’s ability to describe the internal geometry of the nozzles
correctly.
Since this method is based on creating a mold of the internal geometry, each time
a mold of the internal geometry of the nozzle is made, the question about the validity
of the replica should be answered in general. Questions regarding whether the silicone
mold material has been fully extruded to the internal wall surface structure and not only
been extruded to show accumulated dust in the nozzle. Another disadvantage of this
methodology is the risk of breaking the silicone mold replica while in the nozzle, ex-
tracting the mold from the nozzle, and creating a silicone mold blockage. While such
blockage is not impossible to remove, a particular risk of scratching the internal surface
of the nozzle hole is attached, potentially altering the geometry of the nozzle. Hence,
all new hydraulic flow characteristics have to be remade.
In summary of the literature review that has been performed on this topic, it was
chosen that the correct way forward would be the creation of silicone mold replicates,
much with regards to [38]. This methodology was chosen due to its non-destructive
properties, as well as its low cost and ease of execution. While recognizing that this
methodology has certain disadvantages, it was the author’s apparent belief that these
disadvantages could be overcome. Since many of the disadvantages are regarding the
creation of the mold replica, the reader is referred to section 6.4 to see how some of
these disadvantages were overcome.
6.2
Internal geometric parameters of interest
Based on the methodology chosen to measure the internal geometry of the nozzles,
efforts have been made to determine which geometric parameters of the nozzle are of
interest regarding the flow characteristics of the fuel in the hole and downstream of the
spray formation in the chamber. Having made a full description of the effect that inlet
radius and nozzle hole diameter have on the flow characteristics in section 4, a literature
study was made to get a broader understanding of what geometric parameters are of
interest. When analyzing which parameters are of interest, the author has leaned against
43
==PAGE:59==
6.2
Internal geometric parameters of interest
Figure 6.1: Description of the symbols used to describe the geometric parameters of
interest.
literature such as [48], [25],[70]. See Figure 6.1. When analyzing the literature, the
following parameters are of interest.
• Hole diameter As shown in section 4, it was shown that the diameter of the nozzle
has a drastic effect on the mass flow of the fuel and the hydraulic flow character-
ization of the nozzle.
• Circularity and misalignment of hole Based on previous studies, such as [25],
and [70], the so-called hydraulic flip phenomena have been presented. When
evaluating the effect misalignment and circularity have on the flow characteristics,
hydraulic flip, and inclination of the fuel spray to one side in the chamber come
to mind. A hydraulic flip in the nozzle hole can create an uneven mass flux of
the spray. Creating such an un-uniform spray formation in a combustion chamber
may result in poor fuel-air mixing and increased emissions in a potential engine.
• Hole length or L/D Knowing the theory of general fluid mechanics and turbu-
lence theory, [6], it is known that inlet condition has an essential effect on the
development of the flow; one such inlet condition is the length to diameter ratio
of the hole. From theory, it is known - based on the application - that a particular
ratio is needed before a fully developed flow is created. Furthermore, as described
by Nurick et al. [48], increasing the hole length to diameter ratio can decrease the
critical cavitation number. Hence, by having an idea of the length-to-diameter ra-
tio of the hole, one may determine what effect this dimension has on the cavitation
in the nozzle.
• Inlet radius of nozzle hole Based on literature [48], and general fluid mechanical
theory, it is well known that the ”sharpness” of the nozzle hole entrance has the
44
==PAGE:60==
6.3
Experimental setup: Measuring the internal geometric parameters of interest
potential of creating sizeable local pressure drops at the beginning of the hole.
Thus, as described in section 4, cavitation in the nozzle hole may exist at the be-
ginning of the hole. It has been shown that some degree of cavitation in the nozzle
hole is good for increasing the fuel spray angle at the start of the spray, thereby
increasing fuel-air mixing. [70] Therefore, knowing the inlet radius of the nozzle
hole is essential to making any clear statement on the effect the internal geometry
of the nozzles has on the hydraulic flow characterization and spray formation.
• Sac Volume Based on previous studies, among[46], it has been shown that the
size of the sac volume above the nozzle hole entrance influences the discharge
coefficient of the spray. Moreover, when utilizing almost incompressible Diesel
fuel, having a large sac volume can result in a drip of the Diesel in the sac volume
after the injection event. Due to the relatively high compressibility and vapor
pressure of many liquid gaseous fuels, when under high pressure, compressible
tension can result in pressure being accumulated after the pressure source has dis-
appeared. This pressure accumulation means that when the injection event is over
and the nozzle needle has closed, some compressible fuel may have accumulated
some pressure that should be released. This release may result in a small after-
spray in the combustion chamber, especially if the injection is made to a chamber
pressure below the fluid’s vapor pressure. Resulting in increased emissions due
to the low excess air ratio after the combustion of the leading fuel spray.
• Hole K-factor Either manufactured by design or a bi-production of the manufac-
turing methods used to create the nozzle hole, the amount of conical shape the
nozzle can influence the flow of the fuel through the nozzle and, subsequently,
the spray formation. The conical shape of the hole is called the ”K-factor” and is
defined as the K = (dinlet −doutlet)/10. [55]. A converging nozzle hole may help
the fuel reestablish contact with the wall after separation, reducing the risk of a
hydraulic flip in the nozzle hole.
6.3
Experimental setup: Measuring the internal geometric param-
eters of interest
Having previously studied the literature on the methodology for measuring the in-
ternal geometry of Diesel nozzles and established which measuring technique should be
used in this regard, efforts were made to derive an experimental setup to measure these
geometric parameters of interest. These thoughts will be the focus of the following
section.
Firstly, thoughts on producing a silicone mold replica of the internal geometry. Hav-
ing studied [38], it is clearly shown how a tube is inserted into the position where the
needle would be placed under regular operation. Utilizing such a tube and sealing in the

6.4
Experimental procedure for creation of silicone mold
Having described the experimental setup and the design and production of a tube to
create silicone mold replicants of the internal geometry of the nozzle, it became possible
to create a silicone mold. This section explains the procedure used to create a silicone
mold replicant. When creating a silicone mold replica, the following things were used:
• A dispersing gun. [63]
• A mixing nozzle. [66],
• A hypodermic needle nozzle [64]
47
==PAGE:63==
6.4
Experimental procedure for creation of silicone mold
Before filling the custom-designed tube fitting, a tiny portion of silicone mold ma-
terial is dripped onto a piece of paper to reference the silicone material’s drying time.
Having a reference for the drying time, the tube fitting was filled with silicone mold
material without the nozzle attached to the tube. Knowing that the tube is filled with
silicone mold material and that no silicone mold material is present on the outer surface
of the tube, the nozzle is installed on the tube fitting as shown in Figure 2(b). This in-
stallation is done horizontally to ensure that no silicone mold material leaves the seal
in the nozzle needle seat. Having mounted the nozzle and finger tightened the injec-
tor nozzle nut, the hypodermic needle [64] is placed down the filament tube, and extra
silicone mold material is added to force the silicone material to penetrate the injector
nozzle’s single-hole. This extra filament also ensures that the silicone mold material
fills up the sac volume and any cavities in the surface structure of the sac volume. This
extra filament is done until it is visually possible to see silicone material being extruded
from the nozzle hole. The hypodermic needle is slowly removed from the tube while
silicone mold material is added to the tube fitting.
Having filled the injector nozzle and its hole, as well as the tube, the excess silicone
mold material that is extruded out the nozzle single hole is carefully removed by grinding
a piece of paper over the external surface of the injector nozzle. As such, the mold
is ready to dry. Due to the thinness of the silicone material at the beginning of the
drying period of 20 minutes, it is recommended that the nozzle is held in the horizontal
orientation for the first five minutes of the drying process. This orientation ensures that
no material leaves the nozzle single hole.
Dripping a small amount of silicone material on a piece of paper before filling the
tube creates a reference for the dryness of the silicone mold replica. While observing
this reference, it is possible to know when the mold has thoroughly dried.
Knowing the mold has thoroughly dried and is ready for extraction, the tube’s paral-
lel surfaces are installed in a bench vise, and while holding the injector nozzle, to ensure
the nozzle does not rotate while unscrewing the injector nozzle nut, the nut is screwed
off the tube fitting. When the injector nozzle and tube fitting are exposed, the injector
nozzle is carefully and slowly removed from the customized fitting.
Lastly, before installing the mold replica in the Alicona microscope, the mold is
carefully examined in hand under a manual microscope to ensure that no dust or dirt
accumulates on the mold replica. If dust or dirt is present, very carefully blow the replica
with compressed air or very carefully rub the replica with a piece of paper; this may help
remove the dirt on the mold. A relatively clean replica, visually examined for defects,
should overcome any disadvantages of the previously described methodology. As such,
the fitting with the silicone mold replica can be installed in the custom-made dual-axis
head mount. For all scans, it was chosen to use an angle of α = 20◦upward, as shown
in Figure 3(b), as a compromise between getting a scan in horizontal mode while still
getting all details on the curves of the silicone mold replica.
48
==PAGE:64==
6.5
Experimental procedure for utilizing Alicona microscope
6.5
Experimental procedure for utilizing Alicona microscope
Figure 6.4: The two points chosen to define the scanning space in Alicona microscope.
To view the settings set in the Alicona microscope software toolbar, see
appendix C.
The silicone mold scanning process could be initialized after installing the silicone
mold replica in the Alicona microscope. Eight separate surface scans were performed
around the nozzle at different angles to create a full 360-degree 3D surface scan. All
eight scans were made at an angle interval of β = 45◦around the center symmetry axis
of the silicone mold replica. Through pre-trail scans, it has been shown that surface
scans of a silicone mold can accurately capture 50 degrees of the surface around the
symmetry axis. As such, 45 degrees was chosen. This choice made it possible to filter
out non-valid data points at the edges of the surface scans.
For each of the eight surface scans, the saddle point at the rounding of the inlet to the
nozzle hole was identified. This point was defined as the origin of the scanning space and
therefore set in focus. See Figure 6.5. The 3D scanning space is identified by the space
that should be scanned and the lateral and vertical resolution that should be utilized. The
scanning space is defined by specifying two points in the coordinate system of a regular
cuboid, sitting diagonal to each other in all three dimensions. See point (x0, y0, z0), and
(x1, y1, z1) in Figure 6.5. The sac volume’s beginning and the nozzle hole’s ending are
identified to find the X coordinates of the two points. They are determined by utilizing
these two lengths from the point of origin to obtain the two x coordinates x0 ∧x1.
The width of the nozzle hole is roughly identified to find the y-coordinates y0 ∧y1.
Furthermore, the lowest possible focus point is found at the coordinate x0, and the y
coordinate is at y0 to see the negative Z coordinate(z0). Knowing that the highest point
of the scan in the lateral direction is the outlet of the nozzle hole, this point is set in
focus, and the Z coordinate(z1) is identified.
Having identified the scanning space, a vertical resolution of V erticalRes. = 12.00 nm
and a lateral resolution of lateralRes. = 1.2 µm was chosen for all scans performed. A
360-degree 3D reconstruction of the internal geometry of a Diesel injector nozzle could
be generated after data processing the scans in Matlab, using the same procedure for all
49
==PAGE:65==
6.6
Rodrigues’ rotation formula
Figure 6.5: Definition of the scanning space.
eight scans.
6.6
Rodrigues’ rotation formula
Rodrigues’ Rotation formula has to be studied to understand some of the data pro-
cessing techniques used in the next section. The Rodrigues Rotation formula is a pow-
erful tool used within matrix and data rotation. Should the reader already be familiar
with Rodrigues’ Rotation Formula, the reader can skip to the next section.
Figure 6.6: Demonstration of the desired translational/rotational operation.
Assume a vector ⃗u with a random orientation in 3D space. The Rodrigues’ rotation
formula can transform ⃗u to align with vector ⃗v. See Figure 6.6. Generally, to rotate
a vector around an arbitrary axis while preserving its length, one can use Rodrigues’
rotation formula. This formula rotates the vector ⃗u by an angle α around an arbitrary
axis. The equation is given by:
R = E + sin(α)K + (1 −cos(α))K2
(6.1)
where R is the Rodrigues’ rotation matrix, E is the identity matrix, α is the rotation
angle, and K is defined as:
50
==PAGE:66==
6.7




−ν3
ν2
ν3
0
−ν1
−ν2
ν1
0


(6.2)
Here, ⃗ν is the unit vector describing the orientation of the rotation axis. Refer to
vector uvn in Figure 6.6.
Using equation 6.1, it is possible to rotate a set of data points sharing the same central
axis around a specified point and axis to achieve a new orientation. This is typically
done by determining the cross-product of the original and desired central axis vectors,
assuming both axes originate from the same point (the origin), to find the unit vector
describing the rotation axis.
6.7
Data processing of surface scans in Matlab
Having performed surface scans of the silicone mold replica in the Alicona micro-
scope, the data has to be processed to extract the geometric parameters of interest. The
method for this data processing is reported in this section. Figure 6.7 was generated
to get an overview of the whole data processing procedure. See appendix G for a full
detailed view of the Matlab source code. As shown in Figure 6.7, all the data is loaded
Figure 6.7: Flow diagram of the data processing procedure used to process all the data
scans and extract the geometric parameters of interest.
into Matlab. The scans are based on approximately 5-6 million points each, all with an
X, Y, and Z coordinate. Having filtered the data to exclude default measuring points
that include coordinates such as inf and NaN, a scan can be plotted as shown in Figure
51
==PAGE:67==
6.7
Data processing of surface scans in Matlab
(a) α = 0, β = 0

6.8 to derive any useful information from such a scan, it was chosen first to orientate
the scan such that the nozzle hole central symmetry axis is the same as the x-axis.
This process was performed in three steps. Those being (1), based on the angles
set at the custom dual-axis head, the data points were approximately rotated to match
the X-axis. (2) A central symmetry axis for the nozzle hole is determined based on the
nozzle hole. (3) Utilizing Rodrigues’ formula, see section 6.6, all the data points are
rotated such that the x-axis becomes the actual true central symmetry axis of the nozzle
hole.
For the initial process of finding the central symmetry axis for the nozzle hole, the
approximate start and end x coordinate for the nozzle hole was determined using a man-
52
==PAGE:68==
6.7
Data processing of surface scans in Matlab
ual visual inspection of Figure 6.8. The nozzle hole section of the scan was split into
12 sections in the axial direction of the nozzle hole using the approximate start and end
axial coordinate of the hole. For each of the 12 sections, a band fit 2D-dimensional
circle was fitted to the data points in the zy plane to identify a center point for each of
the 12 blocks. See Figure 6.9. Having 12 points that approximate the positions of the
Figure 6.9: Identification of the nozzle hole center points, based on band fits of nozzle
holes 12 blocks. Here, the green points are data points; each of the linear
lines in the Figure is, respectfully, the x, y, and z-axis, while the 3D circles
are band-fitted based on each of the 12 blocks identified in the nozzle hole.
Surface scan being at β = 225 ◦.
central symmetry axis at the middle position of each block, a built-in data processing
function in Matlab, such as: ”Principal component analysis of raw data collapse(PCA)”
[68] could be utilized to find a fit for a linear regression line in space, thereby being able
to describe the central symmetry axis of the nozzle hole. The output of this regression
line is a directional vector, and a point of intersection(⃗PPCA). The data is transformed
such that the coordinates systems point of origin (0, 0, 0), becomes the true point of
intersection for the linear regression, hence ⃗PPCA = (0, 0, 0) . Having performed this
transformation, the axis of rotation between the fitted regression line and the x-axis is
identified. Utilizing Rodrigues’ equation, see section 6.6, the data points were rotated
such that the x-axis becomes the true central symmetry axis for the nozzle hole. See
53
==PAGE:69==

Data processing of surface scans in Matlab
Figure 6.10. Having orientated all the data points such that the x-axis is the actual sym-
(a) Before rotation
(b) After rotation
Figure 6.10: Top view of the surface scan of silicon mold replica, before and after the
rotation of the data points, using Rodrigues’ rotation formula. Thereby
making the x-axis the true symmetry axis of the nozzle hole. Surface scan

metry axis of the nozzle hole, a transformation of the data points from a Cartesian to
a cylindrical coordinate system could be made. This transformation of the data points
makes it possible to collapse the surface scan into a general 2D internal geometry sur-
face wall profile. Using this collapsed profile, a mean radial profile can be identified.
Most of the characteristic geometrical parameters of interest can be derived using this
mean radial profile.
Furthermore, this transformation makes it possible to better filtrate the data for noisy
data points in both the radial/axial and angular directions. As shown in Figure 6.10 and
6.9, invalid data points are present above the scanned surface. Also, due to the curvature
of the nozzle hole, invalid data points are present in the angular edges where the vertical
gradient becomes too large, and the camera’s focus becomes less reliable. Therefore,
a built-in Matlab syntax function ”prctile” is used to filtrate out these points. In this
function, all the angular coordinates are placed in a histogram, and the 2.5 and 97.5
areal lower and upper percentile boundary in the histogram is calculated. Knowing
the upper and lower boundaries, all data points outside these boundaries are filtered
out. See Figure 6.11. The same syntax function was used to filtrate noisy points in
the radial direction. Here the percentile of Q1 = 25 and Q3 = 75 was found. From
these percentiles, the interquartile range can be calculated. [12] Knowing this range,
the upper and lower boundaries were calculated, and data outside these boundaries was
filtered out. See equation 6.3 through 6.5.
IQR = Q3 −Q1
(6.3)
lowerbound = Q1 −1.5IQR
(6.4)

upperbound = Q3 + 1.5IQR
(6.5)
Due to the mean radial profile changing its range, this filtration was chosen to be per-
formed in blocks of 50 in the axial direction of the hole, ensuring that the filtration does
not influence the valid surface points.
After filtrating the data in the angular and radial directions, the scanned surface was
collapsed to create a radial profile of the internal geometry as a function of the z direction
in the nozzle hole. See Figure 6.12. Based on Figure 6.12, it is seen that a relatively
good data fit was obtained.
Regarding the mean radial profile, numerous least square fits were also tried in order
to get better representation of the radial profile. This included spline fit, Fourier series
approximation, and other expressions. This data fitting was not done successful, since
the amount of data points in a scan would require the use of a HPC cluster to compute
the fit in a fashionable time.
The first derivative of the mean radial profile was derived by obtaining any of the
geometric parameters of interest. See Figure 6.14. Based on this Figure, it was possible
to identify clear breaking index points for the beginning and end of (1) the sac volume
circular curve, (2) hole inlet circular curvature, and (3) the nozzle hole.
The following geometric parameters of interest could be derived using these identi-
fied breaking points:
1. Though not perfectly round, a general radius of curvature of the sac volume.
2. A general radius of curvature for the inlet of the nozzle hole.
3. The length of the nozzle hole.
4. The mean diameter of the nozzle and the equivalent variance of this value.
55
==PAGE:71==
6.7
Data processing of surface scans in Matlab
Figure 6.12: Radial profile of a single surface scan of the internal geometry of a Diesel
injector nozzle hole, as a function of the depth direction of the hole. Sur-
face scan being at β = 225 ◦.
5. The K-factor of the hole.
6. The length-to-diameter ratio of the nozzle hole.
To calculate the radius of curvature, a Matlab function from [20] was utilized. This
function can find a data set’s least square circular fit, fitting the radius of curvature and
the center coordinates for the fitted circle. This function was used to determine the sac
volume curvature and the radius of curvature for the nozzle hole inlet.
Using an immature assumption that the nozzle hole inlet radius of curvature will be
the same, an offset in the axial direction between all the surface scans relative to zero
was calculated. This offset was calculated using the difference in the axial coordinate
for all the center points fitted for all the circular fits of the nozzle hole inlet.
As shown in table 6.3, this assumption of the same nozzle hole inlet radius of curva-
ture for all the surface scans is far from ideal. A new mean radial profile was calculated
again, and new circular fits for sac and inlet curvature were performed using the new
position of the surface scans.
To calculate the length of the nozzle hole, the length between the index position
identified in Figure 6.14 was used, knowing that the nozzle hole outlet is not ideally
sharp but instead has a diverging outlet. A discussion of the correct Identification of the

nozzle hole outlet remains reserved. To this end, the point of flow separation between

surface scans is seen as impossible. Also, after identifying the data that describes the

processing method for a different nozzle with a sharp edge outlet will potentially result
in a more definitive length with better reliability and reproducibility between surface
scans at different β angles.

nozzle hole inlet, some reservations should be made. Due to the fragility and flexibility

of the silicone mold replica, it is not entirely possible to exclude the possibility that the
mold was bent after creation. As a result, when evaluating the radius of curvature for the
nozzle hole inlet, it becomes significantly challenging to exclude that the actual radius of
curvature for the nozzle hole inlet has been measured and not the radius where the mold
has been plastically deformed upon removing the mold from the nozzle. Furthermore,
though presumed not to have an effect, the effects gravity has on the silicon mold when
installed in the dual axial head mount have not been investigated. A destructive nozzle
test is thought necessary to get full clearance in this regard.

Due to the long duration of a single surface scan (approximately 4 hours each), it
was not possible from a time perspective to get full nozzle scans with the sac. Surface
scans of the nozzle sac alone could be considered to determine the sac volume. As such,
the author will recommend continuing this work in the future. Thus, extracting the full
radial profile of the sac volume and thereby the sac volume itself.

==PAGE:78==
7
Experimental Setup
7.1
Description of current experimental setup
Figure 7.1: Flow diagram of the experimental setup.
When starting this study, the author had already started developing an injection sys-
tem for injection of alternative fuels in previous projects. [33] [32] [34]. See Figure 7.2.
The injection system is placed in a fume cabinet to handle liquid gaseous fuels safely.
With some changes, this setup was built on many commercially available Common-
rail(CR) injection system principles. Many of the same key components are present,
63
==PAGE:79==
7.1
Description of current experimental setup
Figure 7.2: The experimental rig in the fume cabinet.
such as a fuel tank, fuel filter, high-pressure(HP) pump, injector, HP sensor, and return
64
==PAGE:80==
7.1
Description of current experimental setup
line. The most significant difference is the lack of a CR. Since only one injector was
present in the experimental test stand when designing the injection system, it was found
unnecessary to include a CR for fuel accumulation and fast build-up of pressure was
prioritized.
Due to the relatively high saturation pressure of liquid gaseous fuels, such as liquid
ammonia, a relatively high back pressure of 25 bar is used on the low-pressure side of
the injection system. All components and tubes on the low-pressure side is made to
withstand pressures up to 40 bar. The limiting components are the magnetic solenoid
activated valves. See Figure 7.1.
Helium gas at 25 bar is supplied to the fuel tank through a regulator installed on a
helium tank to pressurize the low-pressure side of the injection system. This helium
tank can withhold 100 bar of pressure.
Utilizing HP NPT series tubing and Cone & threaded fittings on the HP side, this
side can withstand pressure up to approximately 4100 bar; Though, the HP fuel pump,
by sales specification, can only deliver approximately 2600 bars at minimum pump ca-
pacity.
The HP pump is a Maximator G250LVE pneumatic-actuated hydraulic pump. [43].
This pump was originally designed to pump HP water and oil, and thus, it was retrofitted
to withstand liquid gaseous fuels such as liquid ammonia and DME; the sealing gas-
ket was changed in a previous project [33]. See section 3. Compressed air is used
to operate the pump on the main pneumatic side. Having a gearing ratio of 250 to 1,
for one bar of air pressure, 250 bar hydraulic pressure is created on the hydraulic side
of the pump. Using compressed air at maximum 7 bar, a pressure of approximately
1750 bar can be created on the hydraulic side of the pump. Nitrogen pressure at 11 bar
is used to operate the spool valve that directs the compressed air flow in the pump.

to signal the position of the piston. Using these sensors makes it possible to determine
