During previous projects, [33] [32] [34], it was found that under HP loads, the HP
sealing gasket kit in the pump was not always fully tight over time, and some leakage
of Diesel fuel was present over a longer duration in use. To battle this issue, when run-
ning a potentially toxic liquid gaseous fuel such as liquid ammonia, a flexible pressure
hose was mounted on the air supply outlet of the pump and served down to the fume
cabinet ventilation system. To better control the pump’s loading/scavenging process, a
ball valve was added later to lower the fuel intake speed into the displacement cylinder.
66
==PAGE:82==
7.1
Description of current experimental setup
See section 7.2.
The injection chamber in the setup is a rectangular-shaped pressure chamber mounted
with transparent optical acrylic glass windows, 30 mm thick, with the potential to switch
out the windows to metal plates. The chamber can be fitted with optical illuminating
polarized light using the back window to create a uniform light source to light up the
fuel spray. A camera can be mounted to the front window to film the fuel spray injec-
tion event. When the acrylic window is installed, a chamber pressure of 10 bar can be
created inside the chamber. Knowing that acrylic plastic is not very resistant to liquid
ammonia, efforts were made to find alternatives. Different methods were tried to pro-
tect the thick acrylic windows in this regard. First, thin quenched and tempered glass,
commercially used to protect touch screens, was tried. This tempered glass was shown
to be too much of a challenge to cut to shape. Thus, different relatively transparent
tapes were tried. These tapes were unsuccessful since no tape of the correct resistant
material was transparent enough. As such, to this end, it was chosen to laser cut out two
thin(2 mm) acrylic plates and put those between the thicker acrylic windows and the
injection chamber. This way, when the thin acrylic plates become worn, they can easily
be changed for new inserts. Using the metal plate windows, a safe chamber pressure
of 50 bar can be made, thus rendering it possible to simulate the approximate injection
pressure in an internal combustion engine of approximately 30-40 bars gas pressure.
A pressure sensor is mounted on the side of the chamber to measure the pressure in
the chamber. This pressure sensor can measure up to 60 bar of gas pressure. An inlet
port with a solenoid valve is attached to the side for electrical control to fill the pressure
chamber with nitrogen gas pressure. An outlet is present at the bottom of the chamber
to purge the chamber for pressurized gas and liquid gaseous fuels. A solenoid valve can
purge the chamber for pressurized nitrogen gas and liquid gaseous fuels. A relief valve
is installed on the chamber outlet for safety and set to trigger at 40 bar. Since the purged
gasses from the chamber may contain liquid ammonia, all gasses exiting the chamber
are routed to the stripping tank to remove liquid ammonia safely in the experimental test
stand.
Two fuel filters are installed on the setup to protect the pump and injector from
harmful abrasive particles in the fuel. One is installed on the low-pressure sides before
the fuel enters the pump. This filter ensures that any particles from the fuel tank do not
harm the sensitive check ball valves on the pump. See section 7.2. The second fuel filter
is installed after the pump to protect the fine mechanical tolerances in the fuel injector
from the case where the potential default operation of the pump could create abrasive
particles from the pump’s cylinder walls. A 5-micron sintered metal filter is used for the
low-pressure side fuel filter. A 7-micron sintered metal filter is used for the injection
system’s HP side.
Two pressure sensors are installed on the HP side of the injection system. One before
the HP fuel filter and one after the HP fuel filter before the injector fuel intake. The
67
==PAGE:83==
7.1
Description of current experimental setup
pressure sensor mounted before the fuel filter is a Bosch pressure sensor capable of
measuring pressures up to 1500 bar pressure. This pressure sensor has poor linearity, and
some deviation in its reading is presumed present for injection pressures under 500 bar
when assuming a linear gain constant. Thus, the second pressure sensor is mounted after
the fuel filter. This GEMS pressure sensor can measure pressures up to the same range.
This sensor is presumed to have better linearity when using linear gain constants. A
pressure sensor on each side of the HP fuel filter made it possible to observe the pressure
drop over the fuel filter. Thus, it became clear when an unacceptable amount of pressure
drop was observed in the filter, and therefore, the filter inserts had to be cleaned. A quick
Figure 7.5: Sulfuric acid bath in a fume cabinet used to clean the HP sintered filters.
search speculated that nitric acid or sulfuric acid might help clean the sintered stainless
steel sintered filter inserts, with the filter inserts being more resistant to nitric acid than
sulfuric acid. A bath of 99 %wt sulfuric acid was used to clean the filter inserts for five
minutes. This acid was shown to be very efficient at cleaning the filter inserts. However,
after several cleans with the same sulfuric acid, it was found that the filters deteriorated
some over time. Thus, nitric acid may be more promising. Due to the long delivery time
of Nitric acid, this was not tried. Instead, new filter inserts were bought.
A relief valve is mounted on the fuel tank’s return line to safely keep the pressure
under the maximum 40 bar pressure the solenoid valves can withstand.
Two needle valves are mounted on the injection system and used as bleed valves to
purge the system from entrapped helium gas in the fuel supply. One is mounted above
the fuel intake to the pump to ensure proper filament of the pump cylinder with liquid
fuel, and the second is mounted at the outlet return line of the injector. Using the second
bleed valve and the HP needle valve for bypassing the pump, the HP side of the injection
system can be purged from any entrapped helium gas, thereby ensuring that no gas is
present on the HP side. If any gas is present on the HP side, especially in front of the
pump’s outlet check valve, it will not be possible to create high injection pressures due
to the pump only compressing gases and not liquid fuel. This gas will also affect the
sealing capability of the pump’s outlet check valve.
Furthermore, to ensure a steady supply of fuel to the intake of the pump, a 12 mm in
68
==PAGE:84==
7.1
Description of current experimental setup
diameter tube is placed above the pump to accumulate some fuel, thus helping against
vapor lock in the pump. See section 3.1.1 for an explanation of vapor lock.
Due to the great willingness of the reaction between water and anhydrous ammonia,
a safe method for removing liquid ammonia is to react with water to create a low con-
centration of ammonium hydroxide. For this reason, a stripping tank is installed beside
the fume cabinet to handle the waste liquid ammonia fuel from the system. This waste
comes from either the chamber exhaust or the solenoid valve for emptying the injection
system. A tube with many small holes is installed inside the stripping tank, utilizing a
Figure 7.6: Stripping tank for liquid ammonia waste removal.
bored-through tube fitting at the bottom. A steady tap water supply can create a flow
through the bottom and out the top of the stripping tank. Thus, when throttling the
solenoid valve or using the bleed or relief valves, liquid ammonia is evenly distributed
to the water in the stripping tank and reacts with the water to create ammonium hydrox-
ide before being expelled to the sewer.
To ensure high precision weighing of the amount of fuel in the fuel tank, a sub-
assembly for a weighing force transducer was designed and constructed in previous
studies and placed on the floor under the fume cabinet away from the pump’s and injec-

Description of current experimental setup
tor’s vibrations. [34]. This design uses a Siwarex WL 260, SP-S AA 5kg C3 loadcell
Figure 7.7: Weight stand assembly for measuring the weight of fuel tank. [34]
from Siemens A/S. It is capable of measuring the weights up to 5 kg, with a combined
error of ±1 g To ensure correct frictionless contact between the load cell and the metal

the load cell and the metal pole holding the fuel tank.
For assurance of no gas leaks from the fume cabinet and out into the laboratory, an
ammonia gas detector is installed outside on the fume cabinet. It will trigger an alarm
in the laboratory in the event of an ammonia gas detection of more than 50 ppm.
In a previous project, an electrical cabinet was designed and constructed to control
all the electrical components in the experimental test stand .[33]. This electrical cabinet
uses a compact RIO from National Instruments to receive and transmit all digital and
analog input and output signals. This compact RIO consists of an FPGA and a real-time.
The electrical cabinet has two emergency stop bottoms that will cut all power to the
electrical cabinet and test stand in the fume cabinet if one of them is pressed. To ensure

that the test stand is not operated when the laboratory ventilation system is turned off, a

7.2
Cavitation in ball check valve on pump
In the preliminary phase of this project, a default pump operation was observed.
When closing the high-pressure(HP) needle valve for the supply of fuel to the injector,
see Figure 7.2, and while knowing no helium gas was present in the system, it could be
observed that the pump piston was moving, However, despite (1) no leakage of HP fuel
was observed through the HP needle valve on the HP side before and after the pump,
(2) no change in the mass of the fuel tank was observed, it was concluded that the only
reason for the pump’s piston to move, would be if the HP fuel moves through the pump’s
intake ball check valve. To this extent, it was chosen to disassemble the pump to inspect
the pump’s inlet and outlet ball check valves. Upon close inspection of the ball seats in
the inlet and outlet glands, it was observed that cavitation in the ball seats had created
cavitation erosion of the ball seat metal surfaces. See Figure 7.8. As a result, HP fuel
could leak through the inlet ball check valve and back into the low-pressure side of the
injection system. Several initiatives were undertaken to battle this. As described in
section 3.1.1, cavitation occurs due to a local sudden pressure drop of the fuel under
the vapor pressure of the fluid; a pressure drop created by a local increase in velocity
of the fuel. A ball valve was installed on the outlet of the air supply to the pump to
counterbattle this increased velocity, as described in the previous section. The speed at
which the pump can load new fuel can then be regulated using this ball valve.
Another initiative was undertaken to polish up the ball seats in the pump’s inlet and
outlet glands. This polishing was done manually in a metal lathe using sandpaper and a
round bar with angles equal to those of the ball seals. First, a very rough sandpaper(200-
grain) was used. After ensuring that the bottom of all the cracks was reached, a finer
and finer sandpaper ladder was used until a sandpaper of 5000 grain was used to polish
up the ball seat cone-angled surface. This procedure was used for both ball seats. A fur-
ther initiative was undertaken to explore the opportunity of redesigning the inlet gland,
with the intent of smoothing out the sharp corners, thereby smoothing out the velocity
gradients around the inlet gland hole, and also to explore the opportunity of construct-
ing the inlet gland in a more mechanically corrosion-resistant material. The design of
71

.

Figure 7.9: Ball seats after being polished.
.
this inlet gland can be shown in the technical drawing in appendix B. Not knowing
the original material of the inlet gland, to explore the opportunity of making the inlet
gland in a more cavitational corrosion-resistant material, the Pitting resistance equiva-
lent number(PREN) of different metallic materials was studied. It was found here that
the PREN number can somewhat predict how well a material can withstand cavitational-
erosional/corrosional effects. The PREN of a material can be calculated based on the
alloy percent composition of Chromium, Molybdenum, Tungsten, and Nitrogen. See
equation 7.1.
PREN = %Cr + 3.3 × (%Mo + 0.5 × %W) + 16 × %N

A literature review found that a typical PREN over 40 is typically chosen when exposing
components to rough sea conditions. [69]. Due to the HP environment of the inlet gland,
it was found that materials such as a super duplex alloy(DIN 1.4410) would be ideal for
this application. Due to delivery time, this solution was not implemented.

8
Preliminary Results of HSC Recordings of Liquid am-
monia Spray
Before trouble emerged with the pump’s check valves, and after detecting the unsta-
ble injection pressure, see section 9, Mads Carsten Jespersen(Jespersen) (macajo@dtu.dk)
made some preliminary video recordings of liquid ammonia sprays with a high-speed
camera(HSC). Using these recordings, a comparison of the liquid penetration length and
spray angle between Diesel fuel spray on ECN and liquid ammonia fuel sprays can be
made. Based on previous studies, [70] this would give some indication on whether an
increased amount of cavitation is present in a nozzle. The nozzle ”B433206397” was
used to make these recordings.
Based on previous studies performed by Westlye et al. [70], indication pointed to-
ward the cavitation affecting the immediate fuel spray angle and the liquid penetration
length of the spray. Therefore, using some preliminary high-speed video recording,
indications can be made on whether changing the fuel in the nozzle, will change the
internal flow characteristics in a nozzle. From Westlye et al. [70], sprays were made
Figure 8.1: The derived radial profile of liquid ammonia fuel sprays at different injec-
tion and chamber pressures. Credit to Jespersen.
using the ECN spray C and D nozzles. As described in a previous section, the cavitat-
ing spray C nozzle is a nozzle with a no rounding entrance to the nozzle hole, having a
K-factor equal to -2.3. Spray D is a non-cavitating nozzle with rounding in the nozzle
hole entrance, and K-factor equals 0.55. [70] Westlye et al. Showed here that upon the
immediate axial distance from the nozzle tip, Spray C would create a larger spray angle
than Spray D. In contrast, downstream Spray D would create a longer liquid penetration
than Spray D. Although due to a lower area contraction coefficient, Westlye et al. The
shorter liquid penetration length could not be excluded to be a function of the lower area
contraction coefficient.
In comparison between the two sprays in the study of Westlye et al., both sprays
74
==PAGE:90==
were also most identical in their radial profile after a certain axial distance from the
nozzle. Based on the observations made by Westlye et al., they stated that the fuel
sprays inherited good self-similar properties. Based on the overlap of the two sprays,
Westlye et al. identified an axial distance from the nozzle tip where the spray began to
inherit self-similar properties. Naming this value as ”Similarity onset” makes it possible
to identify up until the point where the internal flow characteristics affect the fuel spray.
Where after, no immediate effect of the nozzle flow characteristics is seen to influence
the fuel droplet sizes and vaporization rate.
To study the same trends, Jespersen was able to data process the HSC recordings to
generate sprays with good contrast of the fuel spray. The reader is referred to Jespersen
(macajo@dtu.dk) to get information on the data processing method used to generate the
results in this section. The HSC recording can be seen below in Figure 8.2, 8.3, and
Figure 8.4.
When observing Figure 8.3 and 8.2 and comparing these two conditions with the
conditions shown in Figure 8.4, an apparent inclination of the spray to the left is dis-
played for an injection pressure of 700 bar, but eliminated for a pressure of 1000 bar.
Upon discussion on the cause of this inclination, no clear statement can be made at
present without having access to hydraulic characteristic coefficients.
The critical cavitation number can be found using the cavitation model presented in
section 4. The associated derivation of when the cavitation model ceases to be descrip-
tive can be used to show if this is due to a hydraulic flip or is a situation where poor
nozzle hole geometric outlet conditions are creating minor (0.2 mm) uneven air entrain-
ment into the nozzle. [70] This could potentially create faster fuel droplet acceleration
to one side, thereby making an inclination to one side. Upon evaluation of the discharge
coefficient and area contraction ratio, this inclination may be illuminated further. There-
fore, the author recommends that this study be further studied in the continuation of this
project considering obtained nozzle flow coefficients.
When comparing Figure 8.3 and 8.2, as well as Figure 8.1, several differences can
be observed. Most clearly, it can be observed that the liquid penetration length of the
spray into the chamber is longer for the low chamber pressure(1 bara) than for the high
chamber pressure of (11 baro). This result resembles previous studies of [47], who pre-
dicted the ambient air density in the chamber to affect the fuel spray’s liquid penetration
length.
Furthermore, when comparing Figure 8.3 and 8.2, several key differences exist.
Upon inspection of the video recording, it can be seen that the spray angle at the low
chamber pressure is extensive in the beginning before more fuel is added to the cham-
ber. This swift expansion of the liquid ammonia fuel spray is thought to be a product of
a swift flash boiling event of the fuel when liquid ammonia meets the low pressure in
the chamber.
Another phenomenon also observed in Figure 8.3 and not in Figure 8.2 is the after-

spray of fuel when the nozzle needle has closed for the fuel supply. This after-spray
is thought to be the product of liquid ammonia fuel boiling off in the sac volume of
the nozzle, resulting in an increased pressure in the sac volume that makes the liquid

Fuel injections, as shown in Figure 8.5, were generated to ensure the reproducibility

==PAGE:93==

of the above results. When observing the Liquid penetration of the three sprays tracked

78
==PAGE:94==

thought to be a result of an unreliable injection pressure due to too much fuel leakage

In summary, while certain phenomena can be observed in the HSC recordings and
be explained off the bat by comparing the fuel spray conditions, other phenomena are
more challenging to explain without a better insight into the flow characteristics of the
internal flow in the nozzles. Moreover, due to an unstable injection pressure, the pre-
liminary results shown above cannot be reproduced. Hence, large reservations should
be taken when looking at these results. It is; therefore, the authors recommend further
investigating these preliminary results by studying the hydraulic flow coefficients of the
same nozzle and at the same injection conditions upon the continuation of this project.

9
Modification of Bosch Injector Main Body
9.1
Identification of issue
As previously speculated in section 3, an observation was made on the injection
system. When changing the fuel from a relatively viscous fuel, such as Diesel fuel, into
a fuel with lower viscosity, a decrease in pressure drops over the very fine mechanical
tolerances in the Bosch solenoid injector will exist. Thus, more leakage over these
tolerances will occur.
This diagnostic was made after it was observed that the injection pressure could not
be upheld on the injection system’s high-pressure(HP) side when the HP needle valve
for the return was closed and the HP needle valve for the fuel supply was opened. See
Figure 7.1. This observation was based on the pressure readings of the two HP sensors
Figure 9.1: The unstable injection pressure observed through the injection pressure.
Here, the mean injection pressure is the green curve, and the gray curve
below it is the real measured injection pressure.
on the injection system’s HP side and the proximity sensors installed to tell the pump’s
piston position. When setting a set pressure of 700 bar, it was found that the pressure of
700 bar could only be upheld for very short time periods(≈1.4 s), where after the pump
would run out of displacement volume to pump fuel, and thus had to reload. During the
reloading stroke of the pump, it was observed that the injection pressure on the HP side
would drop approximately 100 bar. As a result, many pressure waves will occur in the
HP tubing, rendering it impossible to make an injection at a clear reliable pressure. See
Figure 9.1.
As previously speculated, due to the significant compressibility of liquid gaseous
fuels, such as liquid ammonia, much of the first part of the pump stroke displacement
volume went to compressing the fuel to the correct set pressure before the check valve
to the HP side was opened, and thus, the pump could pump fuel.
80
==PAGE:96==
9.1
Identification of issue
Before installing the accumulation tube over the pump’s intake, four main potential
diagnostics were identified as the reason for the unreliable injection pressure.
1. Due to the high saturation pressure of liquid ammonia, it was speculated that a
significant pressure drop in the low-pressure side of the injection system would
result in a large quantity of vapor fuel being created during the loading of new
fuel into the pump, thereby creating a low efficient loading process of fuel into
the pump. This process would eventually result in uneven delivery of HP fuel to
the HP side.
2. Due to very low viscous liquid gaseous fuels, the HP needle valves would leak
fuel.
3. Due to low viscous fuels, an increase in leakage through the injector results in
insufficient pumping capacity.
4. Due to the low viscosity of the fuel, the pump’s HP sealing kit would fail, resulting
in small amounts of fuel leaking into the fume cabinet through the pneumatic side
of the pump.
To ensure that the first diagnostics would be eliminated, a fuel-accumulating tube
was installed as described in the previous section 7. Using this tube, assurance could be
made that a sufficient amount of fuel is present to load the pump and that no fluids free
surface is broken in the event that gas bubbles are present in tubing above the pump fuel
intake. Such a gas bubble would result in an inefficient loading process. To this end,
the manual bleed valves over the pump and injector were installed.
To exclude the possibility of the HP needle valve leaking any fuel, the C&T fittings
on the low-pressure side of the HP needle valve, as well as the connection to the injector,
were screwed off. In this setup, the HP tubing from the HP needle valve for circulation
and all the way over to the HP needle valve for the return line was isolated. See Figure
7.1. Based on this setup, it was observed using water-wet pH indicator paper that no
leakage over the HP needle valves was present. Furthermore, having fixed the pump’s
check valves, it could clearly be observed that no movement of the pump’s piston was
present after delivering the set pressure. The pressure in the HP tubing was fully stable
for a long duration of time approximately > 80 s between the reloading strokes in the
pump. In summary, the HP needle valves were sealing tight, and the pump was working
as intended in this setup.
Using the same setup, where the HP tubing was isolated, the weight of the fuel tank
was closely monitored over a longer duration, approximately 30 min, to see if the weight
of the fuel tank would decrease. This would indicate fuel leakage through the pump and
into the fume cabinet since all other fittings were excluded from leaking, using water-
wet pH indicator paper. Based on this observation, no subjective observational decrease
81
==PAGE:97==
9.1
Identification of issue
in the weight of the fuel tank was observed. Hence, this diagnostic was excluded from
being the cause of the unreliable injection pressure.
As a result, it was concluded that diagnostic number (3), an increase in fuel leak-
age over the injector, would be the only logical explanation for the unsteady injection
pressure.
A dummy fuel nozzle was designed and manufactured on a metal lathe to inves-
tigate where the increased fuel leakage occurs in the injector. See Figure 9.2. Using
this dummy nozzle, it could either be confirmed or excluded that the increased leakage
comes from the nozzle needle and its seat in the nozzle. Although this did help lower the
increased leakage, it did not eliminate it. Therefore, it was concluded that the leakage
in the nozzle needle seat was partly due to this leakage over the nozzle needle, but not
entirely because of it.
(a) From below
(b) From the side
(c) Comparison between real nozzle and dummy
nozzle
Figure 9.2: Dummy nozzle installed on the injector to confirm or exclude increased
leakage over the nozzle needle seat.
.
82
==PAGE:98==
9.2
Retrofit design of injector main body
It was then speculated that the injector’s servo valve was potentially not sealing
tight between its bearing ball and ball seat. This was excluded since a leakage over the
injector’s servo valve subassembly would have resulted in the injection opening due to
instability of the force balances in the injector. See section 2.2 for an explanation of the
force balance in the servo valve subassembly.
As a result of this investigation, it was concluded that the increase in fuel leakage
over the injector was partly due to increased leakage over the nozzle needle seat and
partly due to the increased leakage over the injector main body plunger sitting in the
component that constitutes the control volume chamber. See Figure: 3(a) for a cross-
sectional view of the Injectors servo valve subassembly.
9.2
Retrofit design of injector main body
Figure 9.3: The overview of the installed retrofit design of the injector and Common-
rail in the chamber.
To correct this problem, a modified injector design was devised. Knowing that the
increased leakage was due to the injector’s low-pressure side, efforts were made to elim-
inate this side without ruining the injector’s functionality. To this end, it was determined
that sealing up the fuel passage between the injector’s main body’s low-pressure side and
the servo valve’s low-pressure side would be necessary. See Figure 2.2 for an illustra-
tion of this low-pressure passage. To seal the long fuel passage, the end of regular twist
83
==PAGE:99==
9.2
Retrofit design of injector main body
Figure 9.4: The blocked fuel passage in the isolated injector main body part. The
broken-off twist drill ends are shown here using an arrow. This picture
is taken after the adhesive glue has dried up.
drills was broken off its twisted part and used to stuff the fuel passage to ensure no more
than 0.1 mm air passage between the fuel passage wall and the twist drill ends. After
consulting with Loctite A/s, it was found that Adhesive Loctite 648 glue [19] would
withstand the high pressure and be resistant to liquid ammonia. As such, this adhesive
was used to lock the ends of the twisted drills in the fuel passage. After gluing the drills
in place, the adhesive was laid to quenching over a period of more than one week. See
Figure 9.4. Knowing that some pressure difference over the nozzle needle seat is nec-
Figure 9.5: Cross-sectional view of modified injector main body design.
essary to open the injector, the question became how to control the increased pressure
around the main body plunger. To this end, it was determined to drill a hole in the side
of the injector so that a commercially available common rail(CR) can be attached to the
injector’s main body. The fuel in the injector’s main body can bleed into the CR using
this passage. Using the CR’s built-in pressure sensor and proportional servo valve, it is
84
==PAGE:100==
9.2
Retrofit design of injector main body
possible to accurately control the fuel pressure in the injector main body by using the
CR’s servo valve when the pressure in the CR becomes too high. The advantages of us-
ing a commercially available CR include assurance that the CR can withstand high fuel
pressures. A commercially available CR with the part number ”0 445 214 030” was
chosen for this modification. The alloy type of the injector’s main body is unknown. As
Figure 9.6: Cross-sectional view of the injector installed in the chamber.
such, speculation about its strength and integrity became relevant. A structural analysis
of the injector’s main body showed this may be doubtful. For this reason, and to make
it possible to install the CR unto the injector main body, it was necessary to design and
manufacture a new injector plug for installation of the injector into the injection cham-
ber. Due to the materials available, stainless steel 316(SS316) was chosen as the material
for the plug. Using this injector plug and some fine mechanical tolerances between the
injector, injector shell, and plug, it is possible to increase the material thickness and
hopefully make sure that the injector does not plastically deform to the point where the
injector’s functionality is compromised.
This also made it possible to create a design change for the injector plug so that
the nozzle tip of the injector penetrates further into the injection chamber. Hence, when
filming a fuel spray event, the spray is further away from the sealing wall of the injection
chamber, meaning that a better light setup from behind the chamber can be installed,
creating a better focus on the fuel spray.
Due to the limited time period of the project, no time was found to test the modified
injector main body design. This is, therefore, something that is proposed for future
work. To see more detailed technical drawings of the retrofit design of the injector main
body, the reader is referred to appendix B.

10
Modification of Solenoid Head Subassembly
10.1
Identifying issue with solenoid head subassembly
While fixing the pump’s check valves, the original Bosch injector was installed in
the chamber, and while not wanting to waste liquid ammonia, the pump was removed
from the fume cabinet by closing the high-pressure(HP) needle valve for the fuel supply
to the injector. Thus, the injector was exposed to liquid ammonia at vapor pressure over
several days while fixing the ball seats.
After correcting the pump’s check valves, the pump was reinstalled, and attempts
to make injections were unsuccessful. Upon visual inspection of the nozzle tip in the
chamber to see if a fuel spray could be observed, it was observed that black-looking
gunk had accumulated on the nozzle tip of the injector. For this reason, the injector was
uninstalled from the chamber for further inspection. See Figure 10.1. The dirt on the
(a)
(b)
Figure 10.1: Black hardened gunk/dirt accumulated on nozzle tip.
nozzle tip seemed to have flowed through the nozzle and hardened after exposure to the
nitrogen gas in the chamber. To exclude that the dirt was from the chamber and not the
injector, the injector was disassembled to see whether it was also dirty inside. Upon
inspection of the injector, it was confirmed that the black dirt came from the injector
since the same type of black dirt had accumulated on almost all surfaces inside the
injector that is in contact with the fuel. When tracing the black dirt in the injector, the dirt
was traced up to the solenoid head subassembly for the injector’s servo valve. See Figure
10.2. When the solenoid head subassembly was inspected, the plastic cover holding the
solenoid coil inside the solenoid body had chemically decomposed compared to the
plastic from a new solenoid head subassembly. See Figure 10.3. No other defaults were
found when the rest of the injector was further inspected. When factoring in, that all
materials upstream of the injector are made from stainless steel 316(SS316), known to
have ”A” rating compatibility with liquid ammonia, it was concluded that the black dirt
had to come from the injector’s solenoid head subassembly.
To further inspect the solenoid head subassembly, and see what is inside, it was nec-
essary to cut it open destructively since it could not be disassembled without destroying
88
==PAGE:104==
10.1
Identifying issue with solenoid head subassembly
(a) Anchor plate in injector’s servo valve.
(b) close up of Anchor plate.
(c) ball seat in injector’s servo valve.
Figure 10.2: Black hardened gunk/dirt accumulated on anchor in the injectors servo
valve assembly.
(a)
(b)
Figure 10.3: Chemical-worn plastic holding the solenoid coil into the solenoid body
it. Several observations were made after cutting the subassembly. See Figure 10.4 and
10.5. Firstly, it was found that the solenoid body is made of a very fragile sintered
alloy, potentially being powder cast by the Bosch manufacturer. Upon the inspection
described above, it was observed that liquid ammonia was bubbling out of the solenoid
body and vaporizing. This observation showed that the solenoid body absorbed some
liquid ammonia in its pores. Secondly, when observing the cut-open assembly, it was
observed that it contained three O-rings to prevent fuel from leaking out to the over-
head plastic of the solenoid head subassembly. One sealing up against the sleeve and
two sealing between the top hat and the electrical connectors to the solenoid coil. It
is unknown what materials these O-rings were made from; Therefore, replacing these
sealing solutions with others became a priority.
Moreover, since liquid ammonia has
89
==PAGE:105==
10.1
Identifying issue with solenoid head subassembly
Figure 10.4: (left) A new original Bosch solenoid head subassembly. (center) Ex-
tracted solenoid body with epoxy protection coating on plastic. (right)
Cut open solenoid head subassembly.
Figure 10.5: Close-up view of the original solenoid head subassembly cut open.
an excellent potential for cavitation, greater back pressure on the low-pressure side of
the injection system was considered favorable. For this reason, and as explained above,
a better assembly solution for the solenoid head subassembly was necessary.

==PAGE:106==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
Having concluded that the solenoid head subassembly could not withstand the liquid
ammonia, efforts were made to create a better solenoid subassembly. Several prototypes
were designed and manufactured, each showing defects that had to be solved to improve
the subassembly. To better understand the text in this section, it is recommended that the
reader closely studies section 2.2 and the Figures in this section. It may also be relevant
for the reader to extract the CAD technical drawing made of the injector from appendix
B.
10.2.1
Prototype 1
When starting this development process, the first goal was to use the same compo-
nents as in the original solenoid head subassembly but change the sealing O-ring for
the top hat with another O-ring made of ethylene propylene(EPDM), knowing that this
material is compatible with liquid ammonia. Furthermore, wanting to increase the back
pressure on the low-pressure side of the injection system, getting rid of the low-strength
plastic cover on top of the solenoid head subassembly was also considered a priority.
To achieve this prototype design, a new original Bosch solenoid head subassembly was
disassembled by cutting off the plastic of the subassembly in a metal lathe, exposing the
solenoid body sleeve under it. A fine cut in the lathe was made to remove the over-bent
Figure 10.6: Cross-sectional view of the first prototype. Here, it is showing without the
innards of the servo valve and injector main body.
91
==PAGE:107==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
metal from the top of the sleeve. See Figure 10.5. During this process, the solenoid
body and the top hat that holds it in place were extracted. The process was done while
preserving as much of the electrical connection wires to the solenoid coil as possible.
See Figure 10.7.
Having disassembled the original solenoid head subassembly, the author designed
and manufactured low-quality stainless steel clamps to hold down the sleeve to the in-
jector main body and a clamp to hold down the solenoid body and top hat. The idea was
to screw on the clamp for the sleeve and then, using metal thread poles, tighten the two
clamps together to hold the solenoid body and top hat. See Figure 10.6. To make sure
that there is no leakage from the center hole of the solenoid body and up through the
holes in the top hat, intended for the solenoid coils electric connections, a thin 0.5 mm
PTFE plate with the shape of the top surface of the solenoid body was cut in a lathe. See
Figure 10.7.
Figure 10.7: The PTFE sealing gasket plate installed over the solenoid body for the first
prototype.
After consultations with material experts, it was decided that it would be worth trying
to make a thin coating layer of epoxy over the plastic in the solenoid body holding the
coil to protect it from the liquid ammonia. Here, we assumed that the epoxy could
withstand the liquid ammonia. See Figure 10.4.
To connect with the solenoid coil electrically, wires with uninsulated crimp boot-
lace electrical ferrules were soldered onto the connectors sticking up from the solenoid
body, and heat shrink tubing was used to isolate the connections from the top hat. The
prototype was assembled using these manufactured parts and installed in the chamber,
as hinted in Figure 10.6 and 10.8.
When evaluating the prototype, it became clear that the PTFE plate was not sealing
correctly. Liquid ammonia was leaking through the holes in the top hat intended for
solenoid coils electrical connections. This leakage was thought to result from an uneven
compression of the PTFE plate due to an uneven tightening torque on the clamps. Upon
inspection and correction of this torque, it was impossible to eliminate the leakage.
92
==PAGE:108==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
(a) Epoxy coating of plastic in
solenoid body.
(b) Exploded view of parts in solenoid head sub-
assembly.
(c) Prototypes 1 installed in chamber.
Figure 10.8: The first prototype after fabrication.
.
When evaluating the injector’s functionality, the injector was coupled to the elec-
trical cabinet to activate the solenoid. When doing so, it was found that the injector
was clicking, thereby activating, but the magnitude of the sound was less than when
activating an original injector.
Leaving the injector installed in the chamber and with liquid ammonia at vapor pres-
sure overnight, it was found after disassembling the solenoid head subassembly that the
93
==PAGE:109==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
epoxy coating on top of the plastic in the solenoid body had been wholly dissolved, and
flayers of epoxy came off. Thus, this method of protecting the plastic in the solenoid
body was not functional.
10.2.2
Prototype 2
After ascertaining all the dysfunctions of the prototype design, efforts were made to
correct these dysfunctions. These corrections were made with a more severe design, as
shown in Figure 10.9.
Figure 10.9: Cross-sectional view of the second prototype for the solenoid head sub-
assembly.
The prototype had so many problems tightening the PTFE plate that it was decided
to get rid of it and use an O-ring instead. Also, instead of having the O-ring in the top
hat, a new sleeve was designed to seal down around the solenoid body.
To seal off the holes in the top hat, intended for the electrical connections for the
coil, a metal spacer with the same shape as the PTFE plate was manufactured and glued
onto the top face of the solenoid body using epoxy resin. Due to the much lower contact
surface of the epoxy with the liquid ammonia, the hypothesis would be that the epoxy
would not be as affected by the liquid ammonia as in the first prototype.
To protect the plastic in the solenoid body, a thin(0.1 mm) metal plate made from
AISI 1008/AISI 1010 was glued in front of the solenoid to cover up the plastic. This
material was chosen over stainless steel due to its magnetic properties. Different ad-
94
==PAGE:110==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
hesive glues were tried in this glueing process, including Loctite 648 and two epoxies
from 3MTM. See Figure 10.10.
At the origin of this idea, many questions were raised regarding the effect this plate
would have on the solenoid’s magnetic force to lift the anchor subassembly. This effect
was investigated to a small extent and can be seen in section 11. It was also decided to
(a) Isolated top view solenoid body with glued-
on spacer.
(b) Isolated bottom view solenoid body with
glued-on thin metal protection plate.
Figure 10.10: The result of the gluing process of the thin metal protection plate and
spacer on the solenoid body for the second prototype.
design and manufacture a new top hat that enables the use of Swagelok A/S tube fittings
directly onto the top hat. This would make the connection to the low-pressure side much
stronger than in the original Bosch design used in the previous prototype.
To create a more evenly distributed compression of the O-ring between the top hat
and the metal spacer, a new nut and gland around the solenoid sleeve was designed and
manufactured. This gland would be screwed onto the injector’s main body holding the
sleeve, while the nut is screwed onto the new gland and hold the solenoid body and top
hat in place.
All parts in this setup were manufactured in AISI 316 stainless steel.
After manufacturing all the new parts, efforts were made to measure the distance
between the injector’s anchor plate and the surface in the solenoid sleeve, where the
solenoid body rests. This measurement was done to ensure the correct amount of travel
of the anchor subassembly. The risk is that the anchor plate comes too far from the
solenoid body, which would damage the functionality of the solenoid and the risk that
the anchor plate comes too close to the solenoid body, making the anchor plate hit the
thin metal plate glued to the solenoid body.
The prototype was assembled after manufacturing all the parts and ensuring the cor-
rect distances, as hinted in Figure 10.9 and 10.11. Upon assembling this prototype, it
was found that the O-ring replacing the PTFE plate, or the solenoid body sintered ma-
terial, was not tightening correctly. Liquid ammonia was still leaking through the holes
intended for the electrical connections to the solenoid coil. Different tightening torques
on the manufactured nut were applied until 80 Nm. Upon tightening the top hat with this
torque, the top hat began to spin around with the nut while the solenoid body under the
95
==PAGE:111==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
(a) Exploded view of parts in the second prototype.
(b) The second prototype installed in chamber
Figure 10.11: The second prototype exploded view and installation in the chamber.
Here shown with the anti-rotation pins between the spacer and top hat.
top hat remained stationary. This rotation resulted in the electrical connections being
clipped off. This defect was first discovered after having applied the torque. Though
preliminary indications were that the prototype did not leak any liquid ammonia at this
tightening torque when increasing the back pressure to 20 bar.
Two pins were installed in the spacer between the solenoid body, and equivalent
96
==PAGE:112==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
holes were drilled in the top hat to ensure that the solenoid body would always be ori-
ented the same way as the top hat. See Figure 10.12. Upon assembling the prototype
Figure 10.12: The top hat and space between the solenoid body and the top hat. Here
shown with anti-rotation pins installed in the spacer, and holes drilled in

again, it was concluded that it was still not sealing correctly after making different at-
tempts. The injector was then installed in the chamber to evaluate its functionality. Upon
this evaluation, it was found that the injector was not activated when given a signal to
do so.
As a result, the injector was again left in the chamber and exposed to liquid ammonia
at vapor pressure overnight. When disassembling the injector the next day to evaluate
the cause of the dysfunctionality, it was found, as shown in Figure 10.13, that the epoxy
used to glue the then metal plate and the spacer onto the solenoid body had also dis-
solved. See Figure 10.13. As a result, it was concluded that epoxy adhesive glue is too
challenging to work with in this case. Furthermore, there is no way of confirming or
excluding that the leakage observed through the electrical solenoid connections comes
from the sintered solenoid body material or through the O-ring installed between the
spacer and the top hat. Regarding the dysfunctionality of the injector, it could not be ex-
cluded that this could be fixed by installing a thinner shim on top of the solenoid spring,
thereby lowering the preload tension in the spring. See Figure 3(a) for an explanation of
the position of this shim. Since the epoxy had been dissolved, the thin metal protection
plate was not sticking to the solenoid body correctly, so this could not be tested.
10.2.3
Prototype 3
Based on the experience gained from the second prototype and having decided not
to use epoxy, a different way of protecting the plastic in the solenoid body had to be
derived. To this end, several techniques and manufacturing processes were explored on
paper. These techniques included electro-plating the whole solenoid body with a layer
of copper, nickel, and chromium or gold.
Another idea explored was encapsulation of the solenoid body, as shown in Figure
97
==PAGE:113==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 10.13: Yellow dissolved epoxy in the second prototype.
.
10.14. Though having a better concept, this idea was dropped due to the foreseen man-
ufacturing difficulties of the sleeve and the fact that many components from prototype
two would have to be remade to make space for the encapsulation. To this end, a sim-
ilar approach was devised. Using the same thin metal protection plate as in the second
prototype while using a retaining/encapsulating ring around the solenoid body, as well
as a tube going through the center hole of the solenoid body, a similar encapsulation of
the solenoid body can be devised using precision laser welding techniques. See Figure
10.15 for the solenoid body and its encapsulating parts Furthermore, to avoid the sealing
problems experienced in the second prototype, the O-ring groove was designed to seal
around a shaft instead of being compressed between two surfaces. This sealing design
was successful in the second prototype between the sleeve and the solenoid body.
Having evidence that a permanent magnetic material is better at transferring the
magnetic field and, thus, the magnetic force. Much effort was put into deriving the cor-
rect material for the encapsulating parts, wanting stainless and magnetic properties. It
was found that though not having stainless properties, DIN 1.0060 was found to be the
optimal material available without having to spend too much on the delivery of a special
alloy that has both properties, thus counterbalancing preferred properties, cost and avail-
ability. It was found here that the stainless steel AISI 430 in an annulled state would
have both properties but a magnetic permeability five times lower than DIN 1.0060.
[67].
A design was devised to adjust the solenoid spring’s preload tension after the in-
98
==PAGE:114==
10.2
Retrofit design of solenoid head subassembly
Figure 10.14: Cross-sectional view of the solenoid body encapsulation design not im-

jection is installed in the chamber, thus addressing the injector’s dysfunctionality in the
second prototype. See Figure 10.16. This design was achieved by splitting the top hat
part into two components: The spacer that transfers the holding force from the nut down
to the solenoid body and the top hat tube containing the O-ring. By creating a fine thread
between the thick spacer located over the solenoid body and the top hat tube, the tube

solenoid spring, using the long shim as a force transmitter.
Using this design, it will be possible to derive the optimal tension in the spring. Just
enough tension will be present for good injector closure while not having too much force
to overcome the solenoid magnetic force.
In this design, it would be possible to reuse the components from the second proto-
type except the spacer, with a single modification in the nut for the top hat and a single
modification in the sleeve.
As of this thesis’s completion, getting all the parts manufactured in time for testing
was impossible. As a result, it cannot be concluded whether the right design was devised.
It is, therefore, the author’s recommendation to continue on this path in the continuation
of this project. See section 13. The reader is referred to appendix B to see detailed
technical CAD drawings of all the parts in the third prototype.

11
Study on Thin Metal Protection Plate’s Effect on Solenoid
Functionality
As mentioned in the previous section, questions were raised regarding the thin metal
protection plates’ effect on the solenoid coil’s ability to lift the anchor subassembly in
the servo valve. To this extent, it was chosen to get real data on the magnetic force of
the solenoid. This was done through an experimental study. To verify the validity of the
experimental data gained, a qualitative study on the functionality of the magnetic field
around the solenoid body was made. This was done by making a mathematical simula-
tion of the normalized magnetic force density field around the solenoid coil. Based on
the experimental data and the simulation of the qualitative study showing the relation-
ship between the magnetic force, Current, and axial position, extrapolations of the data
were made to predict what material of the metal protection plate is feasible.
11.1
Experimental study of magnetic force from solenoid
In section 2.3 and 2.4, a mathematical model for travel in the injector was devel-
oped. Using this model and the measured values for the spring constants in the injec-
tor’s servo valve, an estimate of the force needed to lift the anchor subassembly in the
servo valve was made. Upon this estimate, it was derived that the magnetic force po-
tentially has to be Fmagnetic ≥58.6 N, when an injection pressure of approximately
300 bar is used. Knowing this estimate, efforts were made to investigate the effect of
the solenoid’s magnetic force when different air gaps and thin metal protection plates
were glued to the solenoid’s plastic. An experimental setup was devised to perform
this investigation, as shown in Figure 11.1. In this setup, a metal milling machine was
Figure 11.1: Experimental setup made to evaluate the electromagnetic force of the
solenoid.
used to mount the solenoid body in its head. See Figure 11.2. The solenoid body could
101
==PAGE:117==
11.1
Experimental study of magnetic force from solenoid
quickly and precisely (within 0.01 mm) be moved up and down using a measuring gauge
on its head. A heavy metal round bar(7.34 kg) together with a weigh scale, was placed
under the solenoid body. The heavy metal round bar was machined to have relatively
(a)
(b)
Figure 11.2: Close-up look on the setup of the weight scale, heavy round metal bar, and
solenoid installed in milling head.
parallel surface ends, and a small hole was drilled in the surface end point towards the
solenoid body. Super glue was used to glue the anchor plate into the round metal bar
hole. Careful efforts were made to make the anchor plate level on the round bar.
Two DC power supplies were connected in parallel to the solenoid coil using its
electrical connectors sticking up from its body. These power supplies could each de-
liver 3 A; thus, a total current of 6 A could be generated. Though this is significantly
less current than the solenoid coil experiences at normal operation, this study was still
considered relevant. Using a voltage of 4 V, the current could be varied to see the effect
of the current going through the solenoid coil.
Three combinations of metal protection plate material and thicknesses were consid-
ered. Each of these four combinations was evaluated at different air gaps and currents.
The result of these combinations can be seen in table 11.1, through 11.1. As shown in

anchor plate were computed to answer this question. See Figure 11.4 Based on the data

gave the best least square fit R-square value. Due to the lack of data for the case with
no plate, no regression line was fitted to this combination. Based on Figure 11.5, when
considering the current profile for typical activation of the injector, it can be concluded
that having a stainless steel protection plate may not be feasible while adding an iron
type plate may most likely be feasible. A reservation should be made when evaluating
the result derived in Figure 11.5. Due to the manual reading of the weight scale together

11.1
Experimental study of magnetic force from solenoid
Figure 11.4: The magnetic force as a function of current at a constant air gap of 0.2 mm
for all combinations.
with the high currents, reservations on the reproducibility of the data extracted should
be made. When running the solenoid coil at high currents, it was observed on the power
supplies that voltage would rapidly increase due to a heating effect in the solenoid. This
would increase the resistance of the solenoid coil. This meant the magnetic force would
increase over time when the current was turned on for longer durations. This would
make it hard to read the weight scale correctly.

11.2
Qualitative study of mathematical simulation on normalized magnetic force
density field
11.2
Qualitative study of mathematical simulation on normalized
magnetic force density field
Having extrapolated the solenoid’s magnetic force based on the current running
through it, it was desired to validate this result. This was done through a qualitative
study of a mathematical simulation showing the normalized magnetic force density field
around the solenoid magnet. The mathematical simulation was done through three steps.
Firstly, a mathematical model was derived to express the solenoid coil as a multi-layer
helix. Secondly, based on this mathematical model of the solenoid coil, the Biot-Savarts
law was used to mathematically simulate the magnetic field around the coil. Thirdly,
a normalized force density field around the coil is derived based on the magnetic field.
[17]
Figure 11.7: Key geometric parameters on the solenoid coil.
11.2.1
Mathematical model of multi-layer solenoid coil
Upon close inspection of Figure 10.5, it can be observed that the solenoid coil in
the solenoid head subassembly is a multi-layer solenoid coil with four layers deep and
nine revolutions per layer. Thus, a total of 36 revolutions is present in the solenoid
coil. To describe this coil mathematically, a regular parametric function of a helix, See
equation 11.2, was chosen as the base of making the mathematical function. Where t is
the parametric value, r is the radius of the helix, and p is the pitch of the helix.

11.2.2
Simulation of the magnetic field around the solenoid coil
Based on the mathematical model for the solenoid coil, the magnetic field around the
solenoid was calculated. This was done using the Biot-Savarts law. [17] See equation
11.11. This equation can describe the magnetic flux density vector field in space around
110
==PAGE:126==
11.2
Qualitative study of mathematical simulation on normalized magnetic force
density field
a given wire wherein an electric current is running. This means that if a solenoid coil is
described through a mathematical parametric function ⃗
h(t), then using this function, it
will be possible to calculate the magnetic flux density vector at any point(⃗P = (x, y, z))
in space around the solenoid coil.
Based on the mathematical model used to describe the solenoid coil, a vector func-
tion to describe the vector between the multi-layer solenoid coil(
⃗
hmulti-layer(t)), and any
point in space, can be derived:
⃗rprime(t, x0, y0, z0) = ⃗P −⃗hmulti-layer(t)
(11.10)
This function can then be used in Biot-Savarats Law to find the magnetic vector field at
point P:
B(x, y, z, I) = µr(⃗P)µ0(⃗P)I

11.2.3
Normalized force density field
Having generated a magnetic flux density force field around the solenoid, efforts
were made to transform this field into a force field. Based on theory from [17], a force
acting on a para-magnet can be described as:
M(x, y, z) = χH =
χ
µ0µr
B(x, y, z)
(11.12a)
m(x, y, z) = V ⃗M(x, y, z)
(11.12b)
F(x, y, z, I) = ∇(m(x, y, z) · B(x, y, z, I))
(11.12c)
Where F(x, y, z, I) is the force field, chi is a material’s magnetic susceptibility, and V
is the volume of the material particle or component susceptible to the magnetic field,
and m is the dipole magnetic moment.
Knowing the relationship between the force and the magnetic field, equation 11.12c
can be rewritten into:
F(x, y, z, I) = χV
µ0µr
∇(B(x, y, z) · B(x, y, z))
(11.13)
From this, the force field can be normalized with the magnetization characteristics(χV ):
F(x, y, z, I)

=
1

(11.14)

current, and relative permeability.

be seen in Figure 11.13. From Figure 11.13, it can be seen that the normalized force
density, and thus also the force acting on the anchor plate, is most likely second-order
polynomial dependent on the current going through the solenoid coil. Large reservations
should be upheld When evaluating Figure 11.12 and 11.13. Due to computational load,
refining the mesh in the simulation was impossible. Thus, a very rough mesh was used
to make this study. Furthermore, from electromagnetic theory, the material’s magnetic
susceptibility does not necessarily have to be a scalar value but can vary with the position
of the material. Also, this dependency does not necessarily have to be linear but can take
on non-linear dependency. [17]

From this study, it can be concluded from Figure 11.12, and 11.12 that the force

this result, it can be seen that introducing the top hat design where the tension in the
solenoid spring can be adjusted was a good design decision. Furthermore, this study
also shows that the previous speculation of the anchor axis creating a more significant

==PAGE:132==
12
Conclusion
At the end of this project, as can be read in the previous sections, an extensive study
of a commercial injection system was undertaken. Especially when it comes to solenoid
injector technology; furthermore, it can be concluded that a comprehensive study of cav-
itation in injection nozzles was conducted, and from this study, new specially designed
nozzles were designed in cooperation with Bosch GmbH as the manufacturer. For this,
a method was fully developed, with some reservations, to investigate and characterize
the internal geometry of nozzle tips and extract geometric key parameters that impact
the flow characteristic of nozzle tips.
After a comprehensive study on the cavitation in injector nozzles was made, it can be
concluded that liquid ammonia fuel sprays were investigated at different injection and
chamber pressure conditions. Upon the evaluation of these fuel sprays, it was concluded
that a more stable injection system was needed, in order to derive flow coefficient that
has to be considered when analyzing the effects seen in the fuel sprays.
Based on an existing injection system, it can be concluded that many of the chal-
lenges faced when switching to a liquid gaseous fuel, such as increased fuel leakage, fuel
compressibility, and chemical compatibility, were investigated. From these studies, so-
lutions were developed to address some of these challenges to create an operational sta-
ble injection system. As can be read in previous sections, it can be concluded that it was
not possible to evaluate some of these solutions, and therefore, assess these solutions is
the primary recommendation for further development of this injection system.
When developing a new solenoid assembly for the injector, it can be concluded that
a qualitative study of the solenoid coil in the injector was investigated. Here, it can be
concluded that a sensitivity analysis on how the axial distance from the solenoid and
the current in the solenoid influence the generated magnetic force available. It can be
concluded here that the distance has a significant effect on the magnetic force available.
Thus, it was a promising design idea to be able to adjust the tension in the solenoid spring
via the top hat. Furthermore, it can be concluded that the current in the solenoid affects
the magnetic force available proportionally to a second-degree polynomial. rendering
the experimental data collected on the magnetic force on the solenoid valid.
117
==PAGE:133==
13
Future work
As described in previous sections of this report, many things were achieved dur-
ing the project’s duration. This project has given great insight into the challenges of
retrofitting a commercially available CR injection system to run on alternative liquid
gaseous fuels such as liquid ammonia. While this project addresses many challenges, it
does not fully declare solutions to every challenge. Thus, several improvements can be
made through the continuation of the project. This section is dedicated to summarizing
some of these improvements.
• Measurement of Customized Diesel nozzles As described in section 5.2, cus-
tomized Diesel nozzles made by Robert Bosch GmBH were ordered. Due to the
delay in delivery time for these nozzles, it was impossible to investigate their
geometry. As such, extensive work must be done to characterize these nozzle’s
geometry upon arrival.
• Creating entirely customized nozzles As also described in section 5.2, 100 noz-
zles without any holes were bought from Bosch GmBH. Having derived man-
ufacturing processes to drill nozzle holes, there is a potential to create entirely
customized Diesel nozzles, where the diameter of the nozzle hole and the radius
of curvature for the hole inlet rounding can be alternated. Such customized noz-
zles will create the opportunity to study the effect of these geometric parameters
on the nozzle’s flow characteristics and, ultimately, the spray formation in the
combustion chamber.
• Evaluation of Prototype 3 As described in section 10.2.3, due to the lack of
time, it was not possible to test the last prototype made to make the servo valves’s
solenoid head subassembly compatible with liquid ammonia. To this end, after
having manufactured all the components needed in this prototype, some work
must be done to test the third prototype regarding its leakage tightness, function-
ality, and liquid ammonia compatibility. Depending on the evaluation of this test-
ing, further efforts can be made to create a better and more accurate simulation of
the magnetic field produced by the solenoid coil, thus making it possible to better
determine how much wiggle room there is in introducing a thin metal protection
plate under the solenoid body.
• Evaluation of modifications made for injector main body As described in sec-
tion 9, due to lack of time, it was not possible to evaluate the design made to
modify the injector main body to create a more stable injection pressure. To this
end, it is recommended that future work be done to assess this design and poten-
tially make changes to the design if needed.
118
==PAGE:134==
• Measurement of flow coefficients After having implemented fully functional
modifications to the injector main body and to the solenoid head subassembly,
thus potentially having a fully functional injection system, it is recommended that
efforts should be made to make a general study of the discharge coefficient using
the nozzle ”B433206397”. This nozzle has previously been used in the evaluation
of its discharge coefficient using Diesel fuel. Hence, using this nozzle to make
the same study using liquid ammonia will make it possible to study the fuel effect
on the flow characteristics in the nozzles. Furthermore, upon the evaluation of
the flow characteristics, it will be possible to explain further some of the effects
seen in the HSC recordings for the liquid ammonia fuel sprays.
• Creating further HSC recordings of fuel sprays After having a fully opera-
tional stable fuel injection system, it is recommended that the liquid ammonia
fuel sprays seen in section 8 be reproduced to confirm their reproducibility and
validity. To this end, it could be considered to obtain an HSC that can generate
the same framerate but at a higher picture resolution.
• Cavitation model as a function of viscous properties Having a method for mea-
suring the discharge coefficient of a nozzle and being able to make entirely cus-
tomized nozzles, it is recommended that efforts are made to create a cavitational
model that not only is a function of areas, hole inlet rounding, and cavitation num-
ber but is also a function of either the viscosity of the fuel or the Reynolds number
of the flow through the nozzle hole.
• Installation of injection system on CVCC Once all the points listed above have
been explored in the future, it will be the recommendation of the author to install
the injection system in a constant volume combustion chamber(CVCC), see sec-
tion 2.6. Using the CVCC, it will be possible to investigate injection, fuel spray,
and combustion of liquid gaseous fuels at much more extreme thermodynamic
conditions.
119
==PAGE:135==

Appendix A
Linkage Between Pressure Drop in Narrow
Passage and Viscosity through Lubrication
Theory
[Disclaimer: It is recommended that the reader reads section 9 before reading
this to see the relevance.] As described in section 3, there is a clear linkage between
the pressure drop in a flow and the viscosity of a fluid in the flow. When factoring
in the solution derived in section 9 for the uncontrolled leakage over the injection, it
seems important to derive a tool that can be used to calculate how low the pressure
may be on the low-pressure side of the injector before leakage over the nozzle needle
seat and injector plunger become too much for the pump to handle. Therefore, a very
brief study on lubrication theory was made. [28] This theory studies the flow through a
narrow passage as shown in Figure A.1. Here a flow with a velocity profile(u(x, y, t) is
Figure A.1: Diagram of the cases studied in lubrication theory.[28] Here u(x, y, t) is a
velocity profile, and h(x, t) is the height of the narrow passage.
observed in a narrow passage, with a height of h(x, t). This passage does not necessarily
have to be constant but can vary with time and position downstream. When setting up
the governing equations for this case, the Naviar Stokes equations can be stated. See
equations below:

==PAGE:171==
File:conversion_between_percent_HE_and_Cd.EES
18-06-2024 13:58:37  Page 1
EES Ver. 11.666: #0780: for use by students and faculty, Technical University of Denmark, Lyngby,Denmark
   1:  $KeyboardUS
   2:  $Unitssystem SI K Pa J mass rad
   3:   
   4:  p = 101e5[Pa]
   5:  T = 298[K]
   6:  p_atm = 1*101325[Pa]
   7:  C_c_sharp = 0.62
   8:  d = 90e-6[m]
   9:  
  10:  //Degree_HE = 5*convert(%,-)
  11:  C_d_after_HE = 0.86
  12:   
  13:  "Conversion"
  14:  p_vapor = p_sat('n-Dodecane',T = T)
  15:  rho = density('n-Dodecane',T = T, P = p)
  16:  
  17:   K_sharp = ((p - p_vapor)/(p - p_atm))
  18:  C_d_sharp = C_c_sharp*K_sharp^(0.5)
  19:  A = (d^2*pi)/4
  20:  v = sqrt((2*p)/rho)
  21:   
  22:  mdot_theoretical = A*rho*v
  23:  C_d_sharp = mdot_sharp/mdot_theoretical
  24:   
  25:   
  26:  Degree_HE = ((mdot_after_HE - mdot_sharp)/mdot_sharp)
  27:   
  28:  C_d_after_HE = mdot_after_HE/mdot_theoretical
  29:  C_d_after_HE = C_c_after_HE*K_sharp^(0.5)
  30:   
  31:  ((1/0.62)^2 - 11.4*(r/d)) = (1/C_c_after_HE)^2
  32:   
  33:  r_d_ratio = r/d

289
function dvdt = dvdt(s, t, forceMagnet , k_under, k_over, s_preload_under , s_preload_over , m_anchor, g, F_pressure,
max_height)
290
291
% Calculate the spring forces
292
F_spring_under = k_under * (s_preload_under + s);
293
F_spring_over = k_over * (s_preload_over - s);
294
295
% Calculate the gravitational force
296
F_g = m_anchor * g;
297
298
% Calculate the magnetic force
299
F_magnet = forceMagnet(t);
300
301
force_static = F_pressure + F_spring_under - F_spring_over - F_g;
302
303
% Define the force balance equation
304
if F_magnet <= abs(force_static) && s == 0
305
dvdt = 0;
306
elseif F_magnet >= abs(force_static) && s == max_height
307
dvdt = 0;
308
else
309
dvdt = (F_pressure + F_spring_under - F_spring_over - F_g + F_magnet) / m_anchor;
310
end
311
end

function dsdt = dsdt(velocity)
314
dsdt = velocity;
315
end

66
dLy = Points(it+1,2) - Points(it,2);
67
dLz = Points(it+1,3) - Points(it,3);
68

70
By = By + mu_0.*Current(j)./(4*pi).*(dLz.*Rx - dLx.*Rz).*R.^(-3);
71
Bz = Bz + mu_0.*Current(j)./(4*pi).*(dLx.*Ry - dLy.*Rx).*R.^(-3);
72
73
end

%% Functions needed to operate the script
444
function by_axis_values = Calculate_b_y_axis_values(pitch, revolutions , layers)
445
point_y = zeros(layers+1,1);
446
point_x = linspace(0,revolutions*2*pi*(1+layers),5);
447
for i = 1:layers+1
448
if mod(i,2)==0
449
point_y(i,1) = pitch*revolutions*2*pi;
450
else
451
point_y(i,1) = 0;
452
end
453
end
454
455
by_axis_values = zeros(layers ,1);
456
for i = 1:layers
457
polycoeff = polyfit(point_x(i:i+1),point_y(i:i+1),1);
458
by_axis_values(i,1) = polycoeff(2);
459
end
460
end
461
462
function [a_n, b_n,f] = fourierCoeffpitch(n, T,pitch,by_axis_values ,revolutions ,layers)
463
% This assumes 'breaks' is the vector where each segment ends, and has one less element than 'slopes' and '
intercepts '
464
% Define the piecewise linear function
465
breaks = revolutions*2*pi.*(1:layers);
466
f = @(x) pitch*x + by_axis_values(1);
% Initialize to the first segment
467
if layers > 1
468
for i = 2:layers
469
if mod(i, 2) == 0
470
f = @(x) f(x) .* (x < breaks(i-1)) + (-pitch * x + by_axis_values(i)) .* (x >= breaks(i-1) & x <
breaks(i));
471
else
472
f = @(x) f(x) .* (x < breaks(i-1)) + (pitch * x + by_axis_values(i)) .* (x >= breaks(i-1) & x <
breaks(i));
473
end
474
end
475
if mod(layers ,2) == 0
476
f = @(x) f(x) .* (x < breaks(end)) + (-pitch * x + by_axis_values(end)) .* (x >= breaks(end));
477
else
478
f = @(x) f(x) .* (x < breaks(end)) + (pitch * x + by_axis_values(end)) .* (x >= breaks(end));
479
end
==PAGE:183==
480
end
481
% Integration for a_n
482
integrand_a = @(x) f(x).*cos(2 * pi * n * x / T);
483
a_n = 2 / T * integral(integrand_a , 0, T);
484
485
% Integration for b_n
486
integrand_b = @(x) f(x).*sin(2 * pi * n * x / T);
487
b_n = 2 / T * integral(integrand_b , 0, T);
488
end
489
490
% Function to calculate Fourier coefficients a_n and b_n
491
function [a_n, b_n] = fourierCoeffR(n, T,coil_wire_dia ,revolutions ,layers)
492
% Function handle for the piecewise function
493
% f = @(x) (x <= 18*pi)*coil_wire_dia(1) + (x > 18*pi & x <= 36*pi)*coil_wire_dia(2) + ...
494
%
(x > 36*pi & x <= 54*pi)*coil_wire_dia(3) + (x > 54*pi)*coil_wire_dia(4);
495
%
496
breaks = revolutions*2*pi.*(1:layers);
497
f = @(t) coil_wire_dia(1);
498
if layers > 1
499
for i = 2:length(coil_wire_dia)
500
f = @(t) f(t).*(t < breaks(i-1)) + coil_wire_dia(i).*(t >= breaks(i-1) & t < breaks(i));
501
end
502
end
503
504
% Integration for a_n
505
integrand_a = @(x) f(x).*cos(2 * pi * n * x / T);
506
a_n = 2 / T * integral(integrand_a , 0, T);
507
508
% Integration for b_n
509
integrand_b = @(x) f(x).*sin(2 * pi * n * x / T);
510
b_n = 2 / T * integral(integrand_b , 0, T);
511
end

513
514
515
function [x_vals, y_vals, z_vals,t_vals,radius_vals ,pitch_vals] = multilayer_coil(L, revolutions , OD, wire_d,
layers,fourier_n_terms ,num_points)
516
517
%Simple calculations for the coil dimensions
518
pitch = L/(revolutions*2*pi);
519
OR = OD/2;
520
521
if layers > 1
522
IR = OR - wire_d - (layers - 1)*((wire_d/2)/tand(30));
523
coil_wire_dia = linspace(IR+(wire_d/2),OR-(wire_d/2),layers);
524
else
525
coil_wire_dia = wire_d/2;
526
end
527
T = revolutions*layers*2*pi;
528
529
530
%% Fourier series fit
531
532
% Calculate coefficients for fourier series to describe the radius of the
533
% wire coil
534
a0_radius = fourierCoeffR(0, T,coil_wire_dia ,revolutions ,layers) / 2;
535
a_radius = zeros(1, fourier_n_terms);
536
b_radius = zeros(1, fourier_n_terms);
537
538
for n = 1:fourier_n_terms
539
[a_radius(n), b_radius(n)] = fourierCoeffR(n, T,coil_wire_dia ,revolutions ,layers);
540
end
541
542
% Define the Fourier series radius function for a single t value
543
fourier_series_single_radius = @(t) a0_radius + sum(a_radius .* cos(2 * pi * (1:fourier_n_terms) * t / T) ...
544
+ b_radius .* sin(2 * pi * (1:fourier_n_terms) * t / T), 2);
545
546
% Evaluate Fourier series at points for an array of t values
547
fourier_series_radius = @(t_vals) arrayfun(fourier_series_single_radius , t_vals);
548
549
%Calculate values for the y axis intersectiing values for the linear fit
550
%lines in piecewise function. To be used in calculating fourier
551
%coefficients in the expression for the pitch control
552
by_axis_values = Calculate_b_y_axis_values(pitch, revolutions , layers);
553
554
% Calculating Fourier coefficients for pitch control function
555
a0_pitch = fourierCoeffpitch(0, T, pitch, by_axis_values , revolutions , layers) / 2; % a0 coefficient
556
a_pitch = zeros(1, fourier_n_terms);
557
b_pitch = zeros(1, fourier_n_terms);
558
for n = 1:fourier_n_terms
559
[a_pitch(n), b_pitch(n), ￿] = fourierCoeffpitch(n, T, pitch, by_axis_values , revolutions , layers);
560
end
==PAGE:184==
561
562
563
% Define the Fourier series pitch function for a single t value
564
fourier_series_single_pitch = @(t) a0_pitch + sum(a_pitch .* cos(2 * pi * (1:fourier_n_terms) * t / T) ...
565
+ b_pitch .* sin(2 * pi * (1:fourier_n_terms) * t / T), 2);
566
567
% Evaluate Fourier series
pitch function at points for an array of t values
568
fourier_series_pitch = @(t_vals) arrayfun(fourier_series_single_pitch , t_vals);
569
570
%Evaluate the fourier series fit functions numerically
571
t_vals = linspace(0, revolutions*layers*2*pi, num_points+2);
572
573
% Evaluate the radius and pitch direction at these t values
574
radius_vals = fourier_series_radius(t_vals);
575
576
% Compute the cumulative pitch values
577
pitch_vals = fourier_series_pitch(t_vals);
578
579
% Compute the coordinates of the helix
580
x_vals = radius_vals .* cos(t_vals);
581
y_vals = radius_vals .* sin(t_vals);
582
z_vals = pitch_vals;
583
584
x_vals = x_vals(2:end-1)';
585
y_vals = y_vals(2:end-1)';
586
z_vals = z_vals(2:end-1)';
587
t_vals = t_vals(2:end-1)';
588
589
end

Library.(newFieldName{i}).hole_linearfit_eval = polyval(Library.(newFieldName{i}).polycoef,Library.(
newFieldName{i}).meanprofileZ_extend);
338
339
% Calculate the R^2 value
340
ytemp = Library.(newFieldName{i}).data_cylindrical(indexpos, 1);
341
yfit = polyval(Library.(newFieldName{i}).polycoef, Library.(newFieldName{i}).data_cylindrical(indexpos, 3));
342
yresid = ytemp - yfit;
343
SSresid = sum(yresid.^2);
344
SStotal = (length(ytemp) - 1) * var(ytemp);
345
Library.(newFieldName{i}).hole_linearfit_R2 = 1 - SSresid/SStotal;
346
347
% Calculate the adjusted R^2 value
348
n = length(ytemp);
349
p = 1; % Number of predictors
350
Library.(newFieldName{i}).hole_linearfit_R2adj = 1 - ((1 - Library.(newFieldName{i}).hole_linearfit_R2) * (n -
1) / (n - p - 1));
351
==PAGE:190==
352
353
%Inlet mean diameter
354
Library.(newFieldName{i}).D0_inlet = 2*polyval(Library.(newFieldName{i}).polycoef,Library.(newFieldName{i}).
meanprofileZ_extend(Library.(newFieldName{i}).end_inletfit));
355
356
%Outlet mean diameter
357
Library.(newFieldName{i}).D0_outlet = 2*polyval(Library.(newFieldName{i}).polycoef,Library.(newFieldName{i}).
meanprofileZ_extend(Library.(newFieldName{i}).end_hole));
358
359
%Kfactor
360
Library.(newFieldName{i}).Kfactor = (Library.(newFieldName{i}).D0_inlet - Library.(newFieldName{i}).D0_outlet)
/10;
361
362
%Mean diameter of hole, use in discharge coefficient
363
Library.(newFieldName{i}).D0_mean = 2*mean(polyval(Library.(newFieldName{i}).polycoef,Library.(newFieldName{i})
.meanprofileZ_extend(Library.(newFieldName{i}).end_inletfit:Library.(newFieldName{i}).end_hole)));
364
365
%variance on mean diameter of hole
366
Library.(newFieldName{i}).D0_var = var(polyval(Library.(newFieldName{i}).polycoef,Library.(newFieldName{i}).
meanprofileZ_extend(Library.(newFieldName{i}).end_inletfit:Library.(newFieldName{i}).end_hole)));
367
368
%% Sort fieldnames in struct
369
% Get the field names of the struct
370
fieldNames = fieldnames(Library.(newFieldName{i}));
371
372
% Sort the field names alphabetically
373
sortedFieldNames = sort(fieldNames);
374
375
% Create a new struct with the sorted field names
376
sortedStruct = struct();
377
for k = 1:length(sortedFieldNames)
378
sortedStruct.(sortedFieldNames{k}) = Library.(newFieldName{i}).(sortedFieldNames{k});
379
end
380
381
% Replace the original struct with the sorted struct
382
Library.(newFieldName{i}) = sortedStruct;
383
end
384
385
Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.data_cylindrical = [];
386
for i = filetorun
387
Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.data_cylindrical = [Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.data_cylindrical;
Library.(newFieldName{i}).data_cylindrical];
388
end
389
Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.data_cylindrical = sortrows(Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.
data_cylindrical ,3);
390
Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.data_cylindrical = removeEveryXthElement(Library.all_cylinderical_data_in_one.
data_cylindrical , 2);
391
392
%% Sort outer struct alphabetic
393
% Get the field names of the outer struct Library
394
outerFieldNames = fieldnames(Library);
395
396
% Sort the outer field names alphabetically
397
sortedOuterFieldNames = sort(outerFieldNames);
398
399
% Create a new struct with the sorted outer field names
400
sortedLibrary = struct();
401
for k = 1:length(sortedOuterFieldNames)
402
sortedLibrary.(sortedOuterFieldNames{k}) = Library.(sortedOuterFieldNames{k});
403
end
404
405
% Replace the original struct with the sorted struct
406
Library = sortedLibrary;

914
function [r,varargout]=circfit(x,y)
915
%CIRCFIT
Least squares fit of X-Y data to a circle.
916
%
R = CIRCFIT(X,Y) returns scalar radius R of a fitted circle. X and Y are 1-D
917
%
arrays of position data in a rectilinear coordinate system. X and Y must be
918
%
the same length and must contain at least three non-colinear points in order
919
%
for a valid solution to be found.
920
%
921
%
[R,ERR] = CIRCFIT(X,Y) additionally returns the scalar root mean squared
922
%
error of the fitted circle radius and center relative to the position data.
923
%
924
%
[R,XC,YC] = CIRCFIT(X,Y) additionally returns the scalar positions, XC and
925
%
YC, of center of the fitted circle.
926
%
==PAGE:198==
927
%
[R,XC,YC,ERR] = CIRCFIT(X,Y) returns both the center positions of the circle
928
%
as well as the root mean squared error.
929
%
930
%
Examples:
931
%
% Fit of just five noisy points
932
%
x1=[1 0 -1 0 1]+0.05*randn(1,5); y1=[0 1 0 -1 0]+0.05*randn(1,5);
933
%
r1=circfit(x1,y1)
934
%
935
%
% CIRCFIT can sometimes perfom poorly with less than 180-degree arc
936
%
t=0:0.1:pi; lt=length(t);
937
%
x2=cos(t)+0.04*randn(1,lt); y2=sin(t)+0.04*randn(1,lt);
938
%
r2_90deg=circfit(x2(1:floor(lt/2)),y2(1:floor(lt/2)))
939
%
r2_180deg=circfit(x2,y2)
940
941
%
Andrew D. Horchler, horchler @ gmail . com, Created 5-12-7
942
%
Revision: 1.3, 4-6-16
943
944
945
% Check inputs
946
if nargout > 4
947
error('circfit:circfit:TooManyOutputs','Too many output arguments.');
948
end
949
950
if
￿isvector(x) ||
￿isfloat(x) ||
￿isreal(x) ||
￿all(isfinite(x))
951
error('circfit:circfit:NonFiniteRealVectorX',...
952
'X must be a finite real vector of floating point numbers.');
953
end
954
if
￿isvector(y) ||
￿isfloat(y) ||
￿isreal(y) ||
￿all(isfinite(y))
955
error('circfit:circfit:NonFiniteRealVectory',...
956
'Y must be a finite real vector of floating point numbers.');
957
end
958
959
lx=length(x);
960
if lx
￿= length(y)
961
error('circfit:circfit:LengthMismatch',...
962
'The vectors X and Y must have the same length.');
963
end
964
if lx < 3
965
error('circfit:circfit:Min3Points',...
966
'The vectors X and Y must contain at least three points.');
967
end
968
x=x(:);
969
y=y(:);
970
971
% Check collinearity , assume with sufficient points, some will be non-collinear
972
if rank(diff([x([1:min(50,lx) 1]) y([1:min(50,lx) 1])])) == 1
973
if lx <= 50 || rank(diff([x y;x(1) y(1)])) == 1
974
error('circfit:circfit:Colinearity',...
975
['The points in vectors X and Y must not all be collinear, or '...
976
'nearly collinear, with each other.']);
977
end
978
end
979
980
xx=x.*x;
981
yy=y.*y;
982
xy=x.*y;
983
xxyy=xx+yy;
984
sx=sum(x);
985
sy=sum(y);
986
sxx=sum(xx);
987
syy=sum(yy);
988
sxy=sum(xy);
989
990
% Solve linear system without inverting
991
% a=[sx sy lx;sxy syy sy;sxx sxy sx]\[sxx+syy;sum(xxyy.*y);sum(xxyy.*x)];
992
[L,U]=lu([sx sy lx;sxy syy sy;sxx sxy sx]);
993
a=U\(L\[sxx+syy;sum(xxyy.*y);sum(xxyy.*x)]);
994
995
xc=0.5*a(1);
% X-position of center of fitted circle
996
yc=0.5*a(2);
% Y-position of center of fitted circle
997
r=sqrt(xc^2+yc^2+a(3)); % Radius of fitted circle
998
999
% Set variable outputs
1000
if nargout > 2
1001
varargout{1}=xc;
1002
varargout{2}=yc;
1003
end
1004
if nargout == 2 || nargout == 4
1005
varargout{nargout -1}=sqrt(mean((sqrt((x-xc).^2+(y-yc).^2)-r).^2));
% RMSE
1006
end
